/></a></td></tr></tbody></table>\n<p><b>Francis Harry Compton Crick</b> <span class="noexcerpt" style="font-size:85%;"><a href="/wiki/Member_of_the_Order_of_Merit" class="mw-redirect" title="Member of the Order of Merit">OM</a>&#32;<a href="/wiki/Fellow_of_the_Royal_Society" title="Fellow of the Royal Society">FRS</a></span><sup id="cite_ref-rsbm_1-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-rsbm-1">&#91;1&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-frs_2-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-frs-2">&#91;2&#93;</a></sup> (8 June 1916 \xe2\x80\x93 28 July 2004) was a <a href="/wiki/British_people" title="British people">British</a> <a href="/wiki/Molecular_biology" title="Molecular biology">molecular biologist</a>, <a href="/wiki/Biophysics" title="Biophysics">biophysicist</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Neuroscience" title="Neuroscience">neuroscientist</a>, most noted for being a co-discoverer of the structure of the <a href="/wiki/DNA" title="DNA">DNA</a> <a href="/wiki/Molecule" title="Molecule">molecule</a> in 1953 with <a href="/wiki/James_Watson" title="James Watson">James Watson</a>.  Together with Watson and <a href="/wiki/Maurice_Wilkins" title="Maurice Wilkins">Maurice Wilkins</a>, he was jointly awarded the 1962 <a href="/wiki/Nobel_Prize_in_Physiology_or_Medicine" title="Nobel Prize in Physiology or Medicine">Nobel Prize in Physiology or Medicine</a> "for their discoveries concerning the <a href="/wiki/Molecular_geometry" title="Molecular geometry">molecular structure</a> of <a href="/wiki/Nucleic_acid" title="Nucleic acid">nucleic acids</a> and its significance for information transfer in living material".<sup id="cite_ref-Obituary_4-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Obituary-4">&#91;4&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-5">&#91;5&#93;</a></sup> The results were based partly on fundamental studies done by <a href="/wiki/Rosalind_Franklin" title="Rosalind Franklin">Rosalind Franklin</a>, <a href="/wiki/Raymond_Gosling" title="Raymond Gosling">Raymond Gosling</a> and Wilkins.\n</p><p>Crick was an important theoretical <a href="/wiki/Molecular_biology" title="Molecular biology">molecular biologist</a> and played a crucial role in research related to revealing the helical structure of DNA. He is widely known for the use of the term "<a href="/wiki/Central_dogma_of_molecular_biology" title="Central dogma of molecular biology">central dogma</a>" to summarize the idea that once information is transferred from nucleic acids (DNA or RNA) to proteins, it cannot flow back to nucleic acids. In other words, the final step in the flow of information from nucleic acids to proteins is irreversible.<sup id="cite_ref-auto_6-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-auto-6">&#91;6&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>During the remainder of his career, he held the post of J.W. Kieckhefer Distinguished Research Professor at the <a href="/wiki/Salk_Institute_for_Biological_Studies" title="Salk Institute for Biological Studies">Salk Institute for Biological Studies</a> in <a href="/wiki/La_Jolla,_California" class="mw-redirect" title="La Jolla, California">La Jolla, California</a>. His later research centered on theoretical <a href="/wiki/Neurobiology" class="mw-redirect" title="Neurobiology">neurobiology</a> and attempts to advance the scientific study of human consciousness. He remained in this post until his death; "he was editing a manuscript on his death bed, a scientist until the bitter end" according to <a href="/wiki/Christof_Koch" title="Christof Koch">Christof Koch</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-7" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-7">&#91;7&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<div id="toc" class="toc"><input type="checkbox" role="button" id="toctogglecheckbox" class="toclevel-2 tocsection-10"><a href="#Creationism"><span class="tocnumber">5.2</span> <span class="toctext">Creationism</span></a></li>\n</ul>\n</li>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-11"><a href="#Views_on_religion"><span class="tocnumber">6</span> <span class="toctext">Views on religion</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-12"><a href="#Directed_panspermia"><span class="tocnumber">7</span> <span class="toctext">Directed panspermia</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-13"><a href="#Neuroscience_and_other_interests"><span class="tocnumber">8</span> <span class="toctext">Neuroscience and other interests</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-14"><a href="#Awards_and_honours"><span class="tocnumber">9</span> <span class="toctext">Awards and honours</span></a>\n<ul>\n<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-15"><a href="#Francis_Crick_Medal_and_Lecture"><span class="tocnumber">9.1</span> <span class="toctext">Francis Crick Medal and Lecture</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-16"><a href="#Francis_Crick_Institute"><span class="tocnumber">9.2</span> <span class="toctext">Francis Crick Institute</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-17"><a href="#Francis_Crick_Graduate_Lectures"><span class="tocnumber">9.3</span> <span class="toctext">Francis Crick Graduate Lectures</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-18"><a href="#Other_honours"><span class="tocnumber">9.4</span> <span class="toctext">Other honours</span></a></li>\n</ul>\n</li>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-19"><a href="#Books"><span class="tocnumber">10</span> <span class="toctext">Books</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-20"><a href="#See_also"><span class="tocnumber">11</span> <span class="toctext">See also</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-21"><a href="#References"><span class="tocnumber">12</span> <span class="toctext">References</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-22"><a href="#Sources"><span class="tocnumber">13</span> <span class="toctext">Sources</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-23"><a href="#Further_reading"><span class="tocnumber">14</span> <span class="toctext">Further reading</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-24"><a href="#External_links"><span class="tocnumber">15</span> <span class="toctext">External links</span></a></li>\n</ul>\n</div>\n\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Early_life_and_education">Early life and education</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Francis_Crick&amp;action=edit&amp;section=1" title="Edit section: Early life and education">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<p>Crick was the first son of Harry Crick (1887\xe2\x80\x931948) and Annie Elizabeth Crick (<a href="/wiki/N%C3%A9e" class="mw-redirect" title="N\xc3\xa9e">n\xc3\xa9e</a> Wilkins; 1879\xe2\x80\x931955). He was born and raised in <a href="/wiki/Weston_Favell" title="Weston Favell">Weston Favell</a>, then a small village near the English town of <a href="/wiki/Northampton" title="Northampton">Northampton</a>, in which Crick\'s father and uncle ran the family\'s boot and shoe factory. His grandfather, <a href="/wiki/Walter_Drawbridge_Crick" title="Walter Drawbridge Crick">Walter Drawbridge Crick</a> (1857\xe2\x80\x931903), an amateur <a href="/wiki/Naturalist" class="mw-redirect" title="Naturalist">naturalist</a>, wrote a survey of local <a href="/wiki/Foraminifera" title="Foraminifera">foraminifera</a> (single-celled <a href="/wiki/Protist" title="Protist">protists</a> with shells), corresponded with <a href="/wiki/Charles_Darwin" title="Charles Darwin">Charles Darwin</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-8" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-8">&#91;8&#93;</a></sup> and had two <a href="/wiki/Gastropod" class="mw-redirect" title="Gastropod">gastropods</a> (snails or slugs) named after him.\n</p><p>At an early age, Francis was attracted to science and what he could learn about it from books. As a child, he was taken to church by his parents. But by about age 12, he said he did not want to go anymore, as he preferred a scientific search for answers over religious belief.<sup id="cite_ref-CrickWMP_9-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-CrickWMP-9">&#91;9&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Walter Crick, his uncle, lived in a small house on the south side of Abington Avenue; he had a shed at the bottom of his little garden where he taught Crick to blow glass, do chemical experiments and to make photographic prints. When he was eight or nine he transferred to the most junior form of the <a href="/wiki/Northampton_Grammar_School" class="mw-redirect" title="Northampton Grammar School">Northampton Grammar School</a>, on the Billing Road. This was about 1.25&#160;mi (2&#160;km) from his home so he could walk there and back, by Park Avenue South and Abington Park Crescent, but he more often went by bus or, later, by bicycle. The teacher \xe2\x80\x93 a Miss Holding \xe2\x80\x93 was an inspired teacher and made everything interesting. The teaching in the higher forms was satisfactory, but not as stimulating. After the age of 14, he was educated at <a href="/wiki/Mill_Hill_School" title="Mill Hill School">Mill Hill School</a> in London (on scholarship), where he studied <a href="/wiki/Mathematics" title="Mathematics">mathematics</a>, <a href="/wiki/Physics" title="Physics">physics</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Chemistry" title="Chemistry">chemistry</a> with his best friend John Shilston. He shared the Walter Knox Prize for Chemistry on Mill Hill School\'s Foundation Day, Friday, 7 July 1933. He declared that his success was inspired by the quality of teaching he received whilst a pupil at Mill Hill.\n</p><p>At the age of 21, Crick earned a <a href="/wiki/Bachelor_of_Science" title="Bachelor of Science">Bachelor of Science</a> degree in physics from <a href="/wiki/University_College,_London" class="mw-redirect" title="University College, London">University College, London</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-CrickWMPc1-2_10-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-CrickWMPc1-2-10">&#91;10&#93;</a></sup> Crick had failed to gain a place at a Cambridge college, probably through failing their requirement for Latin. Crick began his PhD at <a href="/wiki/University_College_London" title="University College London">UCL</a> but was interrupted by World War II. He later became a PhD student<sup id="cite_ref-phd_11-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-phd-11">&#91;11&#93;</a></sup> and Honorary Fellow of <a href="/wiki/Gonville_and_Caius_College,_Cambridge" title="Gonville and Caius College, Cambridge">Gonville and Caius College, Cambridge</a> and mainly worked at the <a href="/wiki/Cavendish_Laboratory" title="Cavendish Laboratory">Cavendish Laboratory</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Medical_Research_Council_(UK)" class="mw-redirect" title="Medical Research Council (UK)">Medical Research Council</a> (MRC) <a href="/wiki/Laboratory_of_Molecular_Biology" title="Laboratory of Molecular Biology">Laboratory of Molecular Biology</a> in Cambridge. He was also an Honorary Fellow of <a href="/wiki/Churchill_College,_Cambridge" title="Churchill College, Cambridge">Churchill College, Cambridge</a> and of University College, London.\n</p><p>Crick began a <a href="/wiki/Doctor_of_Philosophy" title="Doctor of Philosophy">Ph.D.</a> research project on measuring the <a href="/wiki/Viscosity" title="Viscosity">viscosity</a> of water at high temperatures (which he later described as "the dullest problem imaginable"<sup id="cite_ref-CrickWMP13_12-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-CrickWMP13-12">&#91;12&#93;</a></sup>) in the laboratory of physicist <a href="/wiki/Edward_Andrade" title="Edward Andrade">Edward Neville da Costa Andrade</a> at University College London, but with the outbreak of <a href="/wiki/World_War_II" title="World War II">World War II</a> (in particular, an incident during the <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Britain" title="Battle of Britain">Battle of Britain</a> when a bomb fell through the roof of the laboratory and destroyed his experimental apparatus),<sup id="cite_ref-Obituary_4-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Obituary-4">&#91;4&#93;</a></sup> Crick was deflected from a possible career in physics. During his second year as a PhD student, however, he was awarded the Carey Foster Research Prize, a great honour.<sup id="cite_ref-13" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-13">&#91;13&#93;</a></sup> He did postdoctoral work at the <a href="/wiki/Polytechnic_Institute_of_Brooklyn" class="mw-redirect" title="Polytechnic Institute of Brooklyn">Polytechnic Institute of Brooklyn</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-science20.com_14-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-science20.com-14">&#91;14&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>During World War II, he worked for the <a href="/wiki/Admiralty_Research_Laboratory" title="Admiralty Research Laboratory">Admiralty Research Laboratory</a>, from which emerged a group of many notable scientists, including <a href="/wiki/David_Bates_(physicist)" title="David Bates (physicist)">David Bates</a>, <a href="/wiki/Robert_Boyd_(physicist)" title="Robert Boyd (physicist)">Robert Boyd</a>, <a href="/wiki/George_Deacon" title="George Deacon">George Deacon</a>, <a href="/wiki/John_Currie_Gunn" title="John Currie Gunn">John Gunn</a>, <a href="/wiki/Harrie_Massey" title="Harrie Massey">Harrie Massey</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Nevill_Mott" class="mw-redirect" title="Nevill Mott">Nevill Mott</a>; he worked on the design of <a href="/wiki/Electromagnetism" title="Electromagnetism">magnetic</a> and acoustic <a href="/wiki/Naval_mine" title="Naval mine">mines</a>, and was instrumental in designing a new mine that was effective against German <a href="/wiki/Minesweeper_(ship)" class="mw-redirect" title="Minesweeper (ship)">minesweepers</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-15" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-15">&#91;15&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Post-World_War_II_life_and_work">Post-World War II life and work</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Francis_Crick&amp;action=edit&amp;section=2" title="Edit section: Post-World War II life and work">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<p>In 1947, aged 31, Crick began studying biology and became part of an important migration of physical scientists into biology research. This migration was made possible by the newly won influence of physicists such as <a href="/wiki/John_Randall_(physicist)" title="John Randall (physicist)">Sir John Randall</a>, who had helped win the war with inventions such as <a href="/wiki/Radar" title="Radar">radar</a>. Crick had to adjust from the "elegance and deep simplicity" of physics to the "elaborate chemical mechanisms that natural selection had evolved over billions of years." He described this transition as, "almost as if one had to be born again." According to Crick, the experience of learning physics had taught him something important\xe2\x80\x94hubris\xe2\x80\x94and the conviction that since physics was already a success, great advances should also be possible in other sciences such as biology. Crick felt that this attitude encouraged him to be more daring than typical biologists who tended to concern themselves with the daunting problems of biology and not the past successes of physics<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (November 2017)">citation needed</span></a></i>&#93;</sup>.\n</p><p>For the better part of two years, Crick worked on the physical properties of <a href="/wiki/Cytoplasm" title="Cytoplasm">cytoplasm</a> at Cambridge\'s <a href="/wiki/Strangeways_Research_Laboratory" title="Strangeways Research Laboratory">Strangeways Research Laboratory</a>, headed by <a href="/wiki/Honor_Bridget_Fell" class="mw-redirect" title="Honor Bridget Fell">Honor Bridget Fell</a>, with a <a href="/wiki/Medical_Research_Council_(UK)" class="mw-redirect" title="Medical Research Council (UK)">Medical Research Council</a> studentship, until he joined <a href="/wiki/Max_Perutz" title="Max Perutz">Max Perutz</a> and <a href="/wiki/John_Kendrew" title="John Kendrew">John Kendrew</a> at the <a href="/wiki/Cavendish_Laboratory" title="Cavendish Laboratory">Cavendish Laboratory</a>. The Cavendish Laboratory at Cambridge was under the general direction of <a href="/wiki/William_Lawrence_Bragg" class="mw-redirect" title="William Lawrence Bragg">Sir Lawrence Bragg</a>, who had won the <a href="/wiki/Nobel_Prize" title="Nobel Prize">Nobel Prize</a> in 1915 at the age of 25. Bragg was influential in the effort to beat a leading American chemist, <a href="/wiki/Linus_Pauling" title="Linus Pauling">Linus Pauling</a>, to the discovery of <a href="/wiki/DNA" title="DNA">DNA</a>\'s structure (after having been pipped at the post by Pauling\'s success in determining the alpha helix structure of proteins). At the same time Bragg\'s Cavendish Laboratory was also effectively competing with <a href="/wiki/King%27s_College_London" title="King&#39;s College London">King\'s College London</a>, whose Biophysics department was under the direction of Randall. (Randall had refused Crick\'s application to work at King\'s College.) Francis Crick and <a href="/wiki/Maurice_Wilkins" title="Maurice Wilkins">Maurice Wilkins</a> of King\'s College were personal friends, which influenced subsequent scientific events as much as the close friendship between Crick and <a href="/wiki/James_Watson" title="James Watson">James Watson</a>. Crick and Wilkins first met at King\'s College<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (January 2013)">citation needed</span></a></i>&#93;</sup> and not, as erroneously recorded by two authors, at the <a href="/wiki/Admiralty" title="Admiralty">Admiralty</a> during World War II.\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Personal_life">Personal life</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Francis_Crick&amp;action=edit&amp;section=3" title="Edit section: Personal life">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<p>Crick married twice, fathered three children and was the grandfather of six grandchildren; his brother Anthony (born in 1918) predeceased him in 1966.<sup id="cite_ref-16" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-16">&#91;16&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Spouses:\n</p>\n<ul><li>Ruth Doreen Crick, <a href="/wiki/Given_name#Name_at_birth" title="Given name">n\xc3\xa9e</a> Dodd (b. 1913, m. 18 February 1940 \xe2\x80\x93 8 May 1947. d. 2011), became Mrs. James Stewart Potter</li>\n<li><a href="/wiki/Odile_Crick" title="Odile Crick">Odile Crick</a>, <a href="/wiki/Given_name#Name_at_birth" title="Given name">n\xc3\xa9e</a> Speed (b. 11 August 1920, m. 14 August 1949 \xe2\x80\x93 28 July 2004, d. 5 July 2007)</li></ul>\n<p>Children:\n</p>\n<ul><li>Michael Francis Compton (b. 25 November 1940) [by Doreen Crick]</li>\n<li>Gabrielle Anne (b. 15 July 1951) [by Odile Crick]</li>\n<li>Jacqueline Marie-Therese [later Nichols] (b. 12 March 1954, d. 28 February 2011) [by Odile Crick];</li></ul>\n<p>Grandchildren\n</p>\n<ul><li>Alexander (b. March 1974)</li>\n<li>Kindra (b. May 1976)</li>\n<li>Camberley (b. June 1978)</li>\n<li>Francis Henry Riley (b. February 1981), Michael &amp; Barbara Crick\'s four children</li>\n<li>Mark &amp; Nicholas, the late Jacqueline and Christopher Nichols\' children.<sup id="cite_ref-17" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-17">&#91;17&#93;</a></sup></li></ul>\n<p>Crick died of <a href="/wiki/Colorectal_cancer" title="Colorectal cancer">colon cancer</a> on the morning of 28 July 2004<sup id="cite_ref-rsbm_1-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-rsbm-1">&#91;1&#93;</a></sup> at the <a href="/wiki/University_of_California,_San_Diego" title="University of California, San Diego">University of California, San Diego</a> (UCSD) Thornton Hospital in La Jolla; he was <a href="/wiki/Cremated" class="mw-redirect" title="Cremated">cremated</a> and his ashes were scattered into the <a href="/wiki/Pacific_Ocean" title="Pacific Ocean">Pacific Ocean</a>. A public memorial was held on 27 September 2004 at the <a href="/wiki/Salk_Institute_for_Biological_Studies" title="Salk Institute for Biological Studies">Salk Institute</a>, La Jolla, near San Diego, California; guest speakers included James Watson, <a href="/wiki/Sydney_Brenner" title="Sydney Brenner">Sydney Brenner</a>, <a href="/wiki/Alexander_Rich" title="Alexander Rich">Alex Rich</a>, <a href="/wiki/Seymour_Benzer" title="Seymour Benzer">Seymour Benzer</a>, <a href="/wiki/Aaron_Klug" title="Aaron Klug">Aaron Klug</a>, <a href="/wiki/Christof_Koch" title="Christof Koch">Christof Koch</a>, <a href="/wiki/Pat_Churchland" class="mw-redirect" title="Pat Churchland">Pat Churchland</a>, <a href="/wiki/Vilayanur_Ramachandran" class="mw-redirect" title="Vilayanur Ramachandran">Vilayanur Ramachandran</a>, <a href="/wiki/Tomaso_Poggio" title="Tomaso Poggio">Tomaso Poggio</a>, <a href="/wiki/Leslie_Orgel" title="Leslie Orgel">Leslie Orgel</a>, <a href="/wiki/Terry_Sejnowski" title="Terry Sejnowski">Terry Sejnowski</a>, his son Michael Crick, and his youngest daughter Jacqueline Nichols.<sup id="cite_ref-18" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-18">&#91;18&#93;</a></sup> A private memorial for family and colleagues was held on 3 August 2004.\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Research">Research</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Francis_Crick&amp;action=edit&amp;section=4" title="Edit section: Research">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<table class="vertical-navbox nowraplinks plainlist" style="float:right;clear:right;width:22.0em;margin:0 0 1.0em 1.0em;background:#f9f9f9;border:1px solid #aaa;padding:0.2em;border-spacing:0.4em 0;text-align:center;line-height:1.4em;font-size:88%;width:13.0em;background:transparent;"><tbody><tr><td style="padding:0 0.1em 0.4em">\n<div class="NavFrame" style="border:none;padding:0"><div class="NavHead" style="font-size:105%;background:transparent;text-align:left;padding:0.1em 0 0 0.5em;background:#ccf;"><span style="font-size: 120%;"><a href="/wiki/Molecular_Structure_of_Nucleic_Acids:_A_Structure_for_Deoxyribose_Nucleic_Acid" title="Molecular Structure of Nucleic Acids: A Structure for Deoxyribose Nucleic Acid">Double helix</a></span></div><div class="NavContent" style="font-size:105%;padding:0.2em 0 0.4em;text-align:center"><div style="padding:0.4em;"><a href="/wiki/DNA" title="DNA"><img alt="Dna-split2.png" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d5/Dna-split2.png/75px-Dna-split2.png" width="75" height="58" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d5/Dna-split2.png/113px-Dna-split2.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d5/Dna-split2.png/150px-Dna-split2.png 2x" data-file-width="199" data-file-height="155" /></a></div>\n<ul><li><a href="/wiki/William_Astbury" title="William Astbury">William Astbury</a></li>\n<li><a href="/wiki/Oswald_Avery" title="Oswald Avery">Oswald Avery</a></li>\n<li><a href="/wiki/Florence_Bell_(scientist)" title="Florence Bell (scientist)">Florence Bell</a></li>\n<li><a href="/wiki/Lawrence_Bragg" title="Lawrence Bragg">Lawrence Bragg</a></li>\n<li><a class="mw-selflink selflink">Francis Crick</a></li>\n<li><a href="/wiki/Erwin_Chargaff" title="Erwin Chargaff">Erwin Chargaff</a></li>\n<li><a href="/wiki/Michael_Creeth" title="Michael Creeth">Michael Creeth</a></li>\n<li><a href="/wiki/Jerry_Donohue" title="Jerry Donohue">Jerry Donohue</a></li>\n<li><a href="/wiki/Rosalind_Franklin" title="Rosalind Franklin">Rosalind Franklin</a></li>\n<li><a href="/wiki/Raymond_Gosling" title="Raymond Gosling">Raymond Gosling</a></li>\n<li><a href="/wiki/Frederick_Griffith" title="Frederick Griffith">Frederick Griffith</a></li>\n<li><a href="/wiki/John_Masson_Gulland" title="John Masson Gulland">John Masson Gulland</a></li>\n<li><a href="/wiki/Denis_Jordan" title="Denis Jordan">Denis Jordan</a></li>\n<li><a href="/wiki/Phoebus_Levene" title="Phoebus Levene">Phoebus Levene</a></li>\n<li><a href="/wiki/Friedrich_Miescher" title="Friedrich Miescher">Friedrich Miescher</a></li>\n<li><a href="/wiki/Fred_Neufeld" title="Fred Neufeld">Fred Neufeld</a></li>\n<li><a href="/wiki/John_Randall_(physicist)" title="John Randall (physicist)">Sir John Randall</a></li>\n<li><a href="/wiki/Alex_Stokes" title="Alex Stokes">Alex Stokes</a></li>\n<li><a href="/wiki/James_D._Watson" class="mw-redirect" title="James D. Watson">James Watson</a></li>\n<li><a href="/wiki/Maurice_Wilkins" title="Maurice Wilkins">Maurice Wilkins</a></li>\n<li><a href="/wiki/Herbert_Wilson" title="Herbert Wilson">Herbert Wilson</a></li>\n<li><i><a href="/wiki/Photo_51" title="Photo 51">Photo 51</a></i></li></ul></div></div></td>\n</tr><tr><td style="text-align:right;font-size:115%;padding-top: 0.6em;"><div class="plainlinks hlist navbar mini"><ul><li class="nv-view"><a href="/wiki/Template:Single_strand_DNA_discovery" title="Template:Single strand DNA discovery"><abbr title="View this template">v</abbr></a></li><li class="nv-talk"><a href="/wiki/Template_talk:Single_strand_DNA_discovery" title="Template talk:Single strand DNA discovery"><abbr title="Discuss this template">t</abbr></a></li><li class="nv-edit"><a class="external text" href="//en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Template:Single_strand_DNA_discovery&amp;action=edit"><abbr title="Edit this template">e</abbr></a></li></ul></div></td></tr></tbody></table>\n<p>Crick was interested in two fundamental unsolved problems of biology: how molecules make the transition from the non-living to the living, and how the brain makes a conscious mind.<sup id="cite_ref-CrickWMP17_19-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-CrickWMP17-19">&#91;19&#93;</a></sup> He realized that his background made him more qualified for research on the first topic and the field of <a href="/wiki/Biophysics" title="Biophysics">biophysics</a>. It was at this time of Crick\'s transition from physics to biology that he was influenced by both Linus Pauling and <a href="/wiki/Erwin_Schr%C3%B6dinger" title="Erwin Schr\xc3\xb6dinger">Erwin Schr\xc3\xb6dinger</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-CrickWMP18_20-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-CrickWMP18-20">&#91;20&#93;</a></sup> It was clear in theory that <a href="/wiki/Covalent_bond" title="Covalent bond">covalent bonds</a> in biological molecules could provide the structural stability needed to hold <a href="/wiki/Genetics" title="Genetics">genetic</a> information in cells. It only remained as an exercise of experimental biology to discover exactly which molecule was the genetic molecule.<sup id="cite_ref-CrickWMPTalking_21-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-CrickWMPTalking-21">&#91;21&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Judson30_22-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Judson30-22">&#91;22&#93;</a></sup> In Crick\'s view, Charles Darwin\'s theory of <a href="/wiki/Evolution" title="Evolution">evolution</a> by <a href="/wiki/Natural_selection" title="Natural selection">natural selection</a>, <a href="/wiki/Gregor_Mendel" title="Gregor Mendel">Gregor Mendel</a>\'s genetics and knowledge of the molecular basis of genetics, when combined, revealed the secret of life.<sup id="cite_ref-CrickWMP25_23-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-CrickWMP25-23">&#91;23&#93;</a></sup> Crick had the very optimistic view that life would very soon be created in a test tube. However, some people (such as fellow researcher and colleague <a href="/wiki/Esther_Lederberg" title="Esther Lederberg">Esther Lederberg</a>) thought that Crick was unduly optimistic <sup id="cite_ref-24" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-24">&#91;24&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>It was clear that some <a href="/wiki/Macromolecule" title="Macromolecule">macromolecule</a> such as a <a href="/wiki/Protein" title="Protein">protein</a> was likely to be the genetic molecule.<sup id="cite_ref-CrickWMP32_25-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-CrickWMP32-25">&#91;25&#93;</a></sup> However, it was well known that proteins are structural and functional macromolecules, some of which carry out <a href="/wiki/Enzyme" title="Enzyme">enzymatic</a> reactions of cells.<sup id="cite_ref-CrickWMP32_25-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-CrickWMP32-25">&#91;25&#93;</a></sup> In the 1940s, some evidence had been found pointing to another macromolecule, DNA, the other major component of <a href="/wiki/Chromosome" title="Chromosome">chromosomes</a>, as a candidate genetic molecule. In the 1944 <a href="/wiki/Avery-MacLeod-McCarty_experiment" class="mw-redirect" title="Avery-MacLeod-McCarty experiment">Avery-MacLeod-McCarty experiment</a>, <a href="/wiki/Oswald_Avery" title="Oswald Avery">Oswald Avery</a> and his collaborators showed that a heritable <a href="/wiki/Phenotype" title="Phenotype">phenotypic</a> difference could be caused in <a href="/wiki/Bacteria" title="Bacteria">bacteria</a> by providing them with a particular DNA molecule.<sup id="cite_ref-Judson30_22-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Judson30-22">&#91;22&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>However, other evidence was interpreted as suggesting that DNA was structurally uninteresting and possibly just a molecular scaffold for the apparently more interesting protein molecules.<sup id="cite_ref-CrickWMP33_26-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-CrickWMP33-26">&#91;26&#93;</a></sup> Crick was in the right place, in the right frame of mind, at the right time (1949), to join Max Perutz\'s project at the <a href="/wiki/University_of_Cambridge" title="University of Cambridge">University of Cambridge</a>, and he began to work on the <a href="/wiki/X-ray_crystallography" title="X-ray crystallography">X-ray crystallography</a> of proteins.<sup id="cite_ref-CrickWMPCH4_27-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-CrickWMPCH4-27">&#91;27&#93;</a></sup> X-ray crystallography theoretically offered the opportunity to reveal the molecular structure of large molecules like proteins and DNA, but there were serious technical problems then preventing X-ray crystallography from being applicable to such large molecules.<sup id="cite_ref-CrickWMPCH4_27-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-CrickWMPCH4-27">&#91;27&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span id="1949.E2.80.931950"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="1949\xe2\x80\x931950">1949\xe2\x80\x931950</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Francis_Crick&amp;action=edit&amp;section=5" title="Edit section: 1949\xe2\x80\x931950">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<p>Crick taught himself the mathematical theory of X-ray crystallography.<sup id="cite_ref-CrickWMP46_28-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-CrickWMP46-28">&#91;28&#93;</a></sup> During the period of Crick\'s study of <a href="/wiki/X-ray" title="X-ray">X-ray</a> <a href="/wiki/Diffraction" title="Diffraction">diffraction</a>, researchers in the Cambridge lab were attempting to determine the most stable helical conformation of <a href="/wiki/Amino_acid" title="Amino acid">amino acid</a> chains in proteins (the <a href="/wiki/Alpha_helix" title="Alpha helix">alpha helix</a>). Linus Pauling was the first to identify<sup id="cite_ref-29" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-29">&#91;29&#93;</a></sup> the 3.6&#160;amino acids per helix turn ratio of the alpha helix. Crick was witness to the kinds of errors that his co-workers made in their failed attempts to make a correct molecular model of the alpha helix; these turned out to be important lessons that could be applied, in the future, to the helical structure of DNA. For example, he learned<sup id="cite_ref-CrickWMP58_30-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-CrickWMP58-30">&#91;30&#93;</a></sup> the importance of the structural rigidity that <a href="/wiki/Cis-trans_isomerism" class="mw-redirect" title="Cis-trans isomerism">double bonds</a> confer on molecular structures which is relevant both to <a href="/wiki/Peptide_bond" title="Peptide bond">peptide bonds</a> in proteins and the structure of <a href="/wiki/Nucleotide" title="Nucleotide">nucleotides</a> in DNA.\n</p>\n<h3><span id="1951.E2.80.931953:_DNA_structure"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="1951\xe2\x80\x931953:_DNA_structure">1951\xe2\x80\x931953: DNA structure</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Francis_Crick&amp;action=edit&amp;section=6" title="Edit section: 1951\xe2\x80\x931953: DNA structure">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<p>In 1951 and 1952, together with <a href="/wiki/William_Cochran_(physicist)" title="William Cochran (physicist)">William Cochran</a> and Vladimir Vand, Crick assisted in the development of a mathematical theory of X-ray diffraction by a helical molecule.<sup id="cite_ref-31" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-31">&#91;31&#93;</a></sup> This theoretical result matched well with X-ray data for <a href="/wiki/Protein" title="Protein">proteins</a> that contain sequences of amino acids in the alpha helix conformation.<sup id="cite_ref-32" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-32">&#91;32&#93;</a></sup> Helical diffraction theory turned out to also be useful for understanding the structure of DNA.\n</p><p>Late in 1951, Crick started working with James Watson at <a href="/wiki/Cavendish_Laboratory" title="Cavendish Laboratory">Cavendish Laboratory</a> at the <a href="/wiki/University_of_Cambridge" title="University of Cambridge">University of Cambridge</a>, England. Using "<a href="/wiki/Photo_51" title="Photo 51">Photo 51</a>" (the X-ray diffraction results of <a href="/wiki/Rosalind_Franklin" title="Rosalind Franklin">Rosalind Franklin</a> and her graduate student <a href="/wiki/Raymond_Gosling" title="Raymond Gosling">Raymond Gosling</a> of King\'s College London, given to them by Gosling and Franklin\'s colleague Wilkins), Watson and Crick together developed a model for a helical structure of DNA, which they published in 1953.<sup id="cite_ref-33" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-33">&#91;33&#93;</a></sup> For this and subsequent work they were jointly awarded the <a href="/wiki/Nobel_Prize_in_Physiology_or_Medicine" title="Nobel Prize in Physiology or Medicine">Nobel Prize in Physiology or Medicine</a> in 1962 with Wilkins.<sup id="cite_ref-34" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-34">&#91;34&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Profile_35-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Profile-35">&#91;35&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>When Watson came to Cambridge, Crick was a 35-year-old graduate student (due to his work during WWII) and Watson was only 23, but he already had a Ph.D. They shared an interest in the fundamental problem of learning how genetic information might be stored in molecular form.<sup id="cite_ref-CrickWMPgene_36-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-CrickWMPgene-36">&#91;36&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-JudsonOnWatson_37-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-JudsonOnWatson-37">&#91;37&#93;</a></sup> Watson and Crick talked endlessly about DNA and the idea that it might be possible to guess a good molecular model of its structure.<sup id="cite_ref-CrickWMPTalking_21-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-CrickWMPTalking-21">&#91;21&#93;</a></sup> A key piece of experimentally-derived information came from X-ray diffraction images that had been obtained by Wilkins, Franklin, and Gosling. In November 1951, Wilkins came to Cambridge and shared his data with Watson and Crick. <a href="/wiki/Alex_Stokes" title="Alex Stokes">Alexander Stokes</a> (another expert in helical diffraction theory) and Wilkins (both at King\'s College) had reached the conclusion that X-ray diffraction data for DNA indicated that the molecule had a helical structure\xe2\x80\x94but Franklin vehemently disputed this conclusion. Stimulated by their discussions with Wilkins and what Watson learned by attending a talk given by Franklin about her work on DNA, Crick and Watson produced and showed off an erroneous first model of DNA. Their hurry to produce a model of DNA structure was driven in part by the knowledge that they were competing against Linus Pauling. Given Pauling\'s recent success in discovering the Alpha helix, they feared that Pauling might also be the first to determine the structure of DNA.<sup id="cite_ref-WatsonOnPauling_38-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-WatsonOnPauling-38">&#91;38&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Many have speculated about what might have happened had Pauling been able to travel to Britain as planned in May 1952.<sup id="cite_ref-OSUraceforDNA_39-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-OSUraceforDNA-39">&#91;39&#93;</a></sup> As it was, his political activities caused his travel to be restricted by the <a href="/wiki/United_States_government" class="mw-redirect" title="United States government">United States government</a> and he did not visit the UK until later, at which point he met none of the DNA researchers in England. At any rate he was preoccupied with proteins at the time, not DNA.<sup id="cite_ref-OSUraceforDNA_39-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-OSUraceforDNA-39">&#91;39&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-JudsonOnPauling_40-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-JudsonOnPauling-40">&#91;40&#93;</a></sup> Watson and Crick were not officially working on DNA. Crick was writing his Ph.D. thesis; Watson also had other work such as trying to obtain crystals of <a href="/wiki/Myoglobin" title="Myoglobin">myoglobin</a> for X-ray diffraction experiments. In 1952, Watson performed X-ray diffraction on <a href="/wiki/Tobacco_mosaic_virus" title="Tobacco mosaic virus">tobacco mosaic virus</a> and found results indicating that it had helical structure. Having failed once, Watson and Crick were now somewhat reluctant to try again and for a while they were forbidden to make further efforts to find a molecular model of DNA.\n</p>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:DNA_Structure.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/60/DNA_Structure.jpg/220px-DNA_Structure.jpg" width="220" height="357" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/60/DNA_Structure.jpg/330px-DNA_Structure.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/6/60/DNA_Structure.jpg 2x" data-file-width="345" data-file-height="560" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:DNA_Structure.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Diagram that emphasizes the phosphate backbone of DNA. Watson and Crick first made helical models with the phosphates at the centre of the helices.</div></div></div>\n<p>Of great importance to the model building effort of Watson and Crick was Rosalind Franklin\'s understanding of basic chemistry, which indicated that the <a href="/wiki/Hydrophilic" class="mw-redirect" title="Hydrophilic">hydrophilic</a> <a href="/wiki/Phosphate" title="Phosphate">phosphate</a>-containing backbones of the nucleotide chains of DNA should be positioned so as to interact with <a href="/wiki/Water_(molecule)" class="mw-redirect" title="Water (molecule)">water molecules</a> on the outside of the molecule while the <a href="/wiki/Hydrophobic" class="mw-redirect" title="Hydrophobic">hydrophobic</a> bases should be packed into the core. Franklin shared this chemical knowledge with Watson and Crick when she pointed out to them that their first model (from 1951, with the phosphates inside) was obviously wrong.\n</p><p>Crick described what he saw as the failure of Wilkins and Franklin to cooperate and work towards finding a molecular model of DNA as a major reason why he and Watson eventually made a second attempt to do so. They asked for, and received, permission to do so from both William Lawrence Bragg and Wilkins. In order to construct their model of DNA, Watson and Crick made use of information from unpublished X-ray diffraction images of Franklin\'s (shown at meetings and freely shared by Wilkins), including preliminary accounts of Franklin\'s results/photographs of the X-ray images that were included in a written progress report for the King\'s College laboratory of Sir John Randall from late 1952.\n</p><p>It is a matter of debate whether Watson and Crick should have had access to Franklin\'s results without her knowledge or permission, and before she had a chance to <a href="/wiki/Academic_publishing" title="Academic publishing">formally publish</a> the results of her detailed analysis of her X-ray diffraction data which were included in the progress report. However, Watson and Crick found fault in her steadfast assertion that, according to her data, a helical structure was not the only possible shape for DNA\xe2\x80\x94so they had a dilemma. In an effort to clarify this issue, Max Ferdinand Perutz later published what had been in the progress report,<sup id="cite_ref-41" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-41">&#91;41&#93;</a></sup> and suggested that nothing was in the report that Franklin herself had not said in her talk (attended by Watson) in late 1951. Further, Perutz explained that the report was to a Medical Research Council (MRC) committee that had been created in order to "establish contact between the different groups of people working for the Council". Randall\'s and Perutz\'s laboratories were both funded by the MRC.\n</p><p>It is also not clear how important Franklin\'s unpublished results from the progress report actually were for the model-building done by Watson and Crick. After the first crude X-ray diffraction images of DNA were collected in the 1930s, <a href="/wiki/William_Astbury" title="William Astbury">William Astbury</a> had talked about stacks of nucleotides spaced at 3.4 angstr\xc3\xb6m (0.34 nanometre) intervals in DNA. A citation to Astbury\'s earlier X-ray diffraction work was one of only eight references in Franklin\'s first paper on DNA.<sup id="cite_ref-42" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-42">&#91;42&#93;</a></sup> Analysis of Astbury\'s published DNA results and the better X-ray diffraction images collected by Wilkins and Franklin revealed the helical nature of DNA. It was possible to predict the number of bases stacked within a single turn of the DNA helix (10 per turn; a full turn of the helix is 27 angstr\xc3\xb6ms [2.7&#160;nm] in the compact A form, 34 angstr\xc3\xb6ms [3.4&#160;nm] in the wetter B form). Wilkins shared this information about the B form of DNA with Crick and Watson. Crick did not see Franklin\'s B form X-ray images (<a href="/wiki/Photo_51" title="Photo 51">Photo 51</a>) until after the DNA double helix model was published.<sup id="cite_ref-43" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-43">&#91;43&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>One of the few references cited by Watson and Crick when they published their model of DNA was to a published article that included Sven Furberg\'s DNA model that had the bases on the inside. Thus, the Watson and Crick model was not the first "bases in" model to be proposed. Furberg\'s results had also provided the correct orientation of the DNA sugars with respect to the bases. During their model building, Crick and Watson learned that an <a href="/wiki/Antiparallel_(biochemistry)" title="Antiparallel (biochemistry)">antiparallel</a> orientation of the two nucleotide chain backbones worked best to orient the <a href="/wiki/Base_pair" title="Base pair">base pairs</a> in the centre of a double helix. Crick\'s access to Franklin\'s progress report of late 1952 is what made Crick confident that DNA was a double helix with antiparallel chains, but there were other chains of reasoning and sources of information that also led to these conclusions.<sup id="cite_ref-JudsonCh3modeling_44-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-JudsonCh3modeling-44">&#91;44&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>As a result of leaving King\'s College for <a href="/wiki/Birkbeck_College" class="mw-redirect" title="Birkbeck College">Birkbeck College</a>, Franklin was asked by John Randall to give up her work on DNA. When it became clear to Wilkins and the supervisors of Watson and Crick that Franklin was going to the new job, and that Linus Pauling was working on the structure of DNA, they were willing to share Franklin\'s data with Watson and Crick, in the hope that they could find a good model of DNA before Pauling was able. Franklin\'s X-ray diffraction data for DNA and her systematic analysis of DNA\'s structural features was useful to Watson and Crick in guiding them towards a correct molecular model. The key problem for Watson and Crick, which could not be resolved by the data from King\'s College, was to guess how the nucleotide bases pack into the core of the DNA double helix.\n</p>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:DNA_chemical_structure.svg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e4/DNA_chemical_structure.svg/220px-DNA_chemical_structure.svg.png" width="220" height="257" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e4/DNA_chemical_structure.svg/330px-DNA_chemical_structure.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e4/DNA_chemical_structure.svg/440px-DNA_chemical_structure.svg.png 2x" data-file-width="1500" data-file-height="1750" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:DNA_chemical_structure.svg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Diagrammatic representation of some key structural features of DNA. The similar structures of <a href="/wiki/Guanine" title="Guanine">guanine</a>:<a href="/wiki/Cytosine" title="Cytosine">cytosine</a> and <a href="/wiki/Adenine" title="Adenine">adenine</a>:<a href="/wiki/Thymine" title="Thymine">thymine</a> base pairs is illustrated. The base pairs are held together by <a href="/wiki/Hydrogen_bond" title="Hydrogen bond">hydrogen bonds</a>. The phosphate backbones are <a href="/wiki/Antiparallel_(biochemistry)" title="Antiparallel (biochemistry)">anti-parallel</a>.</div></div></div>\n<p>Another key to finding the correct structure of DNA was the so-called <a href="/wiki/Chargaff%27s_rules" title="Chargaff&#39;s rules">Chargaff ratios</a>, experimentally determined ratios of the nucleotide subunits of DNA: the amount of <a href="/wiki/Guanine" title="Guanine">guanine</a> is equal to <a href="/wiki/Cytosine" title="Cytosine">cytosine</a> and the amount of <a href="/wiki/Adenine" title="Adenine">adenine</a> is equal to <a href="/wiki/Thymine" title="Thymine">thymine</a>. A visit by <a href="/wiki/Erwin_Chargaff" title="Erwin Chargaff">Erwin Chargaff</a> to England, in 1952, reinforced the salience of this important fact for Watson and Crick.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (October 2017)">citation needed</span></a></i>&#93;</sup> The significance of these ratios for the structure of DNA were not recognized until Watson, persisting in building structural models, realized that A:T and C:G pairs are structurally similar. In particular, the length of each base pair is the same. Chargaff had also pointed out to Watson that, in the aqueous, saline environment of the cell, the predominant tautomers of the pyrimidine (C and T) bases would be the amine and keto configurations of cytosine and thymine, rather than the imino and enol forms that Crick and Watson had assumed. They consulted <a href="/wiki/Jerry_Donohue" title="Jerry Donohue">Jerry Donohue</a> who confirmed the most likely structures of the nucleotide bases.<sup id="cite_ref-45" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-45">&#91;45&#93;</a></sup> The base pairs are held together by <a href="/wiki/Hydrogen_bond" title="Hydrogen bond">hydrogen bonds</a>, the same non-covalent interaction that stabilize the protein \xce\xb1-helix.  The correct structures were essential for the positioning of the hydrogen bonds.  These insights led Watson to deduce the true biological relationships of the A:T and C:G pairs. After the discovery of the hydrogen bonded A:T and C:G pairs, Watson and Crick soon had their anti-parallel, double helical model of DNA, with the hydrogen bonds at the core of the helix providing a way to "unzip" the two complementary strands for easy <a href="/wiki/DNA_replication" title="DNA replication">replication</a>: the last key requirement for a likely model of the genetic molecule. As important as Crick\'s contributions to the discovery of the double helical DNA model were, he stated that without the chance to collaborate with Watson, he would not have found the structure by himself.<sup id="cite_ref-WMP75_46-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-WMP75-46">&#91;46&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Crick did tentatively attempt to perform some experiments on nucleotide base pairing, but he was more of a theoretical biologist than an experimental biologist. There was another near-discovery of the base pairing rules in early 1952. Crick had started to think about interactions between the bases. He asked John Griffith to try to calculate attractive interactions between the DNA bases from chemical principles and <a href="/wiki/Quantum_mechanics" title="Quantum mechanics">quantum mechanics</a>. Griffith\'s best guess was that A:T and G:C were attractive pairs. At that time, Crick was not aware of Chargaff\'s rules and he made little of Griffith\'s calculations, although it did start him thinking about complementary replication. Identification of the correct base-pairing rules (A-T, G-C) was achieved by Watson "playing" with cardboard cut-out models of the nucleotide bases, much in the manner that Linus Pauling had discovered the protein alpha helix a few years earlier. The Watson and Crick discovery of the DNA double helix structure was made possible by their willingness to combine theory, modelling and experimental results (albeit mostly done by others) to achieve their goal.\n</p><p>The DNA double helix structure proposed by Watson and Crick was based upon "Watson-Crick" bonds between the four bases most frequently found in DNA (A, C, T, G) and RNA (A, C, U, G). However, later research showed that triple-stranded, quadruple-stranded and other more complex DNA molecular structures required <a href="/wiki/Hoogsteen_base_pair" title="Hoogsteen base pair">Hoogsteen base pairing</a>. The entire field of <a href="/wiki/Synthetic_biology" title="Synthetic biology">synthetic biology</a> began with work by researchers such as <a href="/w/index.php?title=Erik_T._Kool&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" class="new" title="Erik T. Kool (page does not exist)">Erik T. Kool</a>, in which bases other than A, C, T and G are used in a synthetic DNA. In addition to synthetic DNA there are also attempts to construct synthetic <a href="/wiki/Codons" class="mw-redirect" title="Codons">codons</a>, synthetic <a href="/wiki/Endonucleases" class="mw-redirect" title="Endonucleases">endonucleases</a>, synthetic proteins and synthetic <a href="/wiki/Zinc_fingers" class="mw-redirect" title="Zinc fingers">zinc fingers</a>. Using synthetic DNA, instead of there being 4<sup>3</sup> codons, if there are n new bases there could be as many as n<sup>3</sup> codons. Research is currently being done to see if codons can be expanded to more than 3 bases. These new codons can code for new amino acids. These synthetic molecules can be used not only in medicine, but in creation of new materials.<sup id="cite_ref-Simon,_Matthew_2005_47-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Simon,_Matthew_2005-47">&#91;47&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>The discovery was made on 28 February 1953; the first Watson/Crick paper appeared in <i><a href="/wiki/Nature_(journal)" title="Nature (journal)">Nature</a></i> on 25 April 1953. Sir Lawrence Bragg, the director of the <a href="/wiki/Cavendish_Laboratory" title="Cavendish Laboratory">Cavendish Laboratory</a>, where Watson and Crick worked, gave a talk at <a href="/wiki/Guy%27s_Hospital" title="Guy&#39;s Hospital">Guy\'s Hospital</a> Medical School in London on Thursday 14 May 1953 which resulted in an article by Ritchie Calder in the <i><a href="/wiki/News_Chronicle" title="News Chronicle">News Chronicle</a></i> of London, on Friday 15 May 1953, entitled "Why You Are You. Nearer Secret of Life." The news reached readers of <i><a href="/wiki/The_New_York_Times" title="The New York Times">The New York Times</a></i> the next day; Victor K. McElheny, in researching his biography, "Watson and DNA: Making a Scientific Revolution", found a clipping of a six-paragraph New York Times article written from London and dated 16 May 1953 with the headline "Form of \'Life Unit\' in Cell Is Scanned." The article ran in an early edition and was then pulled to make space for news deemed more important. (<i>The New York Times</i> subsequently ran a longer article on 12 June 1953). The university\'s undergraduate newspaper <i><a href="/wiki/Varsity_(Cambridge)" title="Varsity (Cambridge)">Varsity</a></i> also ran its own short article on the discovery on Saturday 30 May 1953. Bragg\'s original announcement of the discovery at a <a href="/wiki/Solvay_conference" class="mw-redirect" title="Solvay conference">Solvay conference</a> on <a href="/wiki/Proteins" class="mw-redirect" title="Proteins">proteins</a> in <a href="/wiki/Belgium" title="Belgium">Belgium</a> on 8 April 1953 went unreported by the British press.\n</p><p>In a seven-page, handwritten letter<sup id="cite_ref-48" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-48">&#91;48&#93;</a></sup> to his son at a British boarding school on 19 March 1953 Crick explained his discovery, beginning the letter <i>"My Dear Michael, Jim Watson and I have probably made a most important discovery..."</i>.<sup id="cite_ref-sonletter_49-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-sonletter-49">&#91;49&#93;</a></sup> The letter was put up for auction at <a href="/wiki/Christie%27s" title="Christie&#39;s">Christie\'s</a> New York on 10 April 2013 with an estimate of $1 to $2 million, eventually selling for $6,059,750, the largest amount ever paid for a letter at auction.<sup id="cite_ref-letterauction_50-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-letterauction-50">&#91;50&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p><a href="/wiki/Sydney_Brenner" title="Sydney Brenner">Sydney Brenner</a>, <a href="/wiki/Jack_D._Dunitz" title="Jack D. Dunitz">Jack Dunitz</a>, <a href="/wiki/Dorothy_Hodgkin" title="Dorothy Hodgkin">Dorothy Hodgkin</a>, <a href="/wiki/Leslie_Orgel" title="Leslie Orgel">Leslie Orgel</a>, and <a href="/w/index.php?title=Beryl_M._Oughton&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" class="new" title="Beryl M. Oughton (page does not exist)">Beryl M. Oughton</a>, were some of the first people in April 1953 to see the model of the structure of <a href="/wiki/DNA" title="DNA">DNA</a>, constructed by Crick and Watson; at the time they were working at <a href="/wiki/University_of_Oxford" title="University of Oxford">Oxford University</a>\'s Chemistry Department. All were impressed by the new DNA model, especially Brenner who subsequently worked with Crick at <a href="/wiki/University_of_Cambridge" title="University of Cambridge">Cambridge</a> in the Cavendish Laboratory and the new <a href="/wiki/Laboratory_of_Molecular_Biology" title="Laboratory of Molecular Biology">Laboratory of Molecular Biology</a>. According to the late Dr. Beryl Oughton, later Rimmer, they all travelled together in two cars once Dorothy Hodgkin announced to them that they were off to Cambridge to see the model of the structure of DNA.<sup id="cite_ref-51" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-51">&#91;51&#93;</a></sup> Orgel also later worked with Crick at the <a href="/wiki/Salk_Institute_for_Biological_Studies" title="Salk Institute for Biological Studies">Salk Institute for Biological Studies</a>.\n</p>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:DNA_Model_Crick-Watson.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/50/DNA_Model_Crick-Watson.jpg/220px-DNA_Model_Crick-Watson.jpg" width="220" height="293" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/50/DNA_Model_Crick-Watson.jpg/330px-DNA_Model_Crick-Watson.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/50/DNA_Model_Crick-Watson.jpg/440px-DNA_Model_Crick-Watson.jpg 2x" data-file-width="960" data-file-height="1280" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:DNA_Model_Crick-Watson.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Crick and Watson DNA model built in 1953, was <a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="http://www.farooqhussain.org/projects/">reconstructed</a> largely from its original pieces in 1973 and donated to the <a href="/wiki/Science_Museum_(London)" class="mw-redirect" title="Science Museum (London)">National Science Museum</a> in London.</div></div></div>\n<p>Soon after Crick\'s death, there have been allegations about him having used LSD when he came to the idea of the helix structure of the DNA.<sup id="cite_ref-52" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-52">&#91;52&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-53" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-53">&#91;53&#93;</a></sup> While he almost certainly did use LSD, it is unlikely that he was doing that as early as 1953.<sup id="cite_ref-54" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-54">&#91;54&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Molecular_biology">Molecular biology</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Francis_Crick&amp;action=edit&amp;section=7" title="Edit section: Molecular biology">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<p>In 1954, at the age of 37, Crick completed his Ph.D. thesis: "<i>X-Ray Diffraction: Polypeptides and Proteins</i>" and received his degree. Crick then worked in the laboratory of <a href="/wiki/David_Harker" title="David Harker">David Harker</a> at <a href="/wiki/Polytechnic_University_of_New_York" class="mw-redirect" title="Polytechnic University of New York">Brooklyn Polytechnic Institute</a>, where he continued to develop his skills in the analysis of <a href="/wiki/X-ray_crystallography" title="X-ray crystallography">X-ray diffraction</a> data for proteins, working primarily on <a href="/wiki/Ribonuclease" title="Ribonuclease">ribonuclease</a> and the mechanisms of <a href="/wiki/Protein_synthesis" class="mw-redirect" title="Protein synthesis">protein synthesis</a>. David Harker, the American X-ray crystallographer, was described as "the John Wayne of crystallography" by Vittorio Luzzati, a crystallographer at the Centre for Molecular Genetics in Gif-sur-Yvette near Paris, who had worked with Rosalind Franklin.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (March 2015)">citation needed</span></a></i>&#93;</sup>\n</p><p>After the discovery of the double helix model of DNA, Crick\'s interests quickly turned to the biological implications of the structure. In 1953, Watson and Crick published another article in <i>Nature</i> which stated: "it therefore seems likely that the precise sequence of the bases is the code that carries the genetical information".<sup id="cite_ref-55" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-55">&#91;55&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:101px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Collagentriplehelix.png" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d1/Collagentriplehelix.png/99px-Collagentriplehelix.png" width="99" height="20" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d1/Collagentriplehelix.png/149px-Collagentriplehelix.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d1/Collagentriplehelix.png/198px-Collagentriplehelix.png 2x" data-file-width="1600" data-file-height="320" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Collagentriplehelix.png" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Collagen triple helix.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (October 2011)">citation needed</span></a></i>&#93;</sup></div></div></div>\n<p>In 1956, Crick and Watson speculated on the structure of small <a href="/wiki/Virus" title="Virus">viruses</a>. They suggested that spherical viruses such as <a href="/wiki/Tomato_bushy_stunt_virus" title="Tomato bushy stunt virus">Tomato bushy stunt virus</a> had icosahedral symmetry and were made from 60 identical subunits.<sup id="cite_ref-56" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-56">&#91;56&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>After his short time in New York, Crick returned to Cambridge where he worked until 1976, at which time he moved to <a href="/wiki/California" title="California">California</a>. Crick engaged in several X-ray diffraction collaborations such as one with <a href="/wiki/Alexander_Rich" title="Alexander Rich">Alexander Rich</a> on the structure of <a href="/wiki/Collagen" title="Collagen">collagen</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-57" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-57">&#91;57&#93;</a></sup> However, Crick was quickly drifting away from continued work related to his expertise in the interpretation of X-ray diffraction patterns of proteins.\n</p><p><a href="/wiki/George_Gamow" title="George Gamow">George Gamow</a> established a group of scientists interested in the role of <a href="/wiki/RNA" title="RNA">RNA</a> as an intermediary between DNA as the genetic storage molecule in the <a href="/wiki/Nucleus_(cell)" class="mw-redirect" title="Nucleus (cell)">nucleus</a> of cells and the synthesis of proteins in the <a href="/wiki/Cytoplasm" title="Cytoplasm">cytoplasm</a> (the <a href="/wiki/RNA_Tie_Club" title="RNA Tie Club">RNA Tie Club</a>). It was clear to Crick that there had to be a code by which a short sequence of nucleotides would specify a particular <a href="/wiki/Amino_acid" title="Amino acid">amino acid</a> in a newly synthesized protein. In 1956, Crick wrote an informal paper about the <a href="/wiki/Genetic_code" title="Genetic code">genetic coding</a> problem for the small group of scientists in Gamow\'s RNA group.<sup id="cite_ref-58" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-58">&#91;58&#93;</a></sup> In this article, Crick reviewed the evidence supporting the idea that there was a common set of about 20 amino acids used to synthesize proteins. Crick proposed that there was a corresponding set of small "adaptor molecules" that would <a href="/wiki/Hydrogen_bond" title="Hydrogen bond">hydrogen bond</a> to short sequences of a nucleic acid, and also link to one of the amino acids. He also explored the many theoretical possibilities by which short nucleic acid sequences might code for the 20 amino acids.\n</p>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:3d_tRNA.png" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f1/3d_tRNA.png/220px-3d_tRNA.png" width="220" height="219" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f1/3d_tRNA.png/330px-3d_tRNA.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f1/3d_tRNA.png/440px-3d_tRNA.png 2x" data-file-width="1061" data-file-height="1056" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:3d_tRNA.png" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Molecular model of a <a href="/wiki/TRNA" class="mw-redirect" title="TRNA">tRNA</a> molecule.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (October 2011)">citation needed</span></a></i>&#93;</sup> Crick predicted that such adaptor molecules might exist as the links between <a href="/wiki/Codon" class="mw-redirect" title="Codon">codons</a> and <a href="/wiki/Amino_acid" title="Amino acid">amino acids</a>.</div></div></div>\n<p>During the mid-to-late 1950s Crick was very much intellectually engaged in sorting out the mystery of how proteins are synthesized. By 1958, Crick\'s thinking had matured and he could list in an orderly way all of the key features of the protein synthesis process:<sup id="cite_ref-auto_6-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-auto-6">&#91;6&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<ul><li>genetic information stored in the sequence of DNA molecules</li>\n<li>a "messenger" RNA molecule to carry the instructions for making one protein to the cytoplasm</li>\n<li>adaptor molecules ("they might contain nucleotides") to match short sequences of nucleotides in the RNA messenger molecules to specific amino acids</li>\n<li>ribonucleic-protein complexes that catalyse the assembly of amino acids into proteins according to the messenger RNA</li></ul>\n<p>The adaptor molecules were eventually shown to be <a href="/wiki/TRNA" class="mw-redirect" title="TRNA">tRNAs</a> and the catalytic "ribonucleic-protein complexes" became known as <a href="/wiki/Ribosome" title="Ribosome">ribosomes</a>. An important step was later realization (in 1960) that the <a href="/wiki/Messenger_RNA" title="Messenger RNA">messenger RNA</a> was not the same as the <a href="/wiki/Ribosomal_RNA" title="Ribosomal RNA">ribosomal RNA</a>. None of this, however, answered the fundamental theoretical question of the exact nature of the genetic code. In his 1958 article, Crick speculated, as had others, that a triplet of nucleotides could code for an amino acid. Such a code might be "degenerate", with 4\xc3\x974\xc3\x974=64 possible triplets of the four nucleotide subunits while there were only 20 amino acids. Some amino acids might have multiple triplet codes. Crick also explored other codes in which, for various reasons, only some of the triplets were used, "magically" producing just the 20 needed combinations.<sup id="cite_ref-59" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-59">&#91;59&#93;</a></sup> Experimental results were needed; theory alone could not decide the nature of the code. Crick also used the term "<a href="/wiki/Central_dogma_of_molecular_biology" title="Central dogma of molecular biology">central dogma</a>" to summarize an idea that implies that genetic information flow between macromolecules would be essentially one-way:\n</p>\n<dl><dd><b>DNA \xe2\x86\x92 RNA \xe2\x86\x92 Protein</b></dd></dl>\n<p>Some critics thought that by using the word "dogma", Crick was implying that this was a rule that could not be questioned, but all he really meant was that it was a compelling idea without much solid evidence to support it. In his thinking about the biological processes linking DNA genes to proteins, Crick made explicit the distinction between the materials involved, the energy required, and the information flow. Crick was focused on this third component (information) and it became the organizing principle of what became known as molecular biology. Crick had by this time become a highly influential theoretical molecular biologist.\n</p><p>Proof that the genetic code is a degenerate triplet code finally came from genetics experiments, some of which were performed by Crick.<sup id="cite_ref-60" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-60">&#91;60&#93;</a></sup> The details of the code came mostly from work by <a href="/wiki/Marshall_Warren_Nirenberg" title="Marshall Warren Nirenberg">Marshall Nirenberg</a> and others who synthesized synthetic RNA molecules and used them as templates for <i><a href="/wiki/In_vitro" title="In vitro">in vitro</a></i> protein synthesis.<sup id="cite_ref-61" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-61">&#91;61&#93;</a></sup> Nirenberg first announced his results to a small audience in Moscow at a 1961 conference. Crick\'s reaction was to invite Nirenberg to deliver his talk to a larger audience.<sup id="cite_ref-62" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-62">&#91;62&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Controversy">Controversy</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Francis_Crick&amp;action=edit&amp;section=8" title="Edit section: Controversy">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<table class="plainlinks metadata ambox ambox-content" role="presentation"><tbody><tr><td class="mbox-image"><div style="width:52px"><img alt="Ambox scales.svg" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5c/Ambox_scales.svg/40px-Ambox_scales.svg.png" width="40" height="40" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5c/Ambox_scales.svg/60px-Ambox_scales.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5c/Ambox_scales.svg/80px-Ambox_scales.svg.png 2x" data-file-width="620" data-file-height="620" /></div></td><td class="mbox-text"><div class="mbox-text-span">This article <b>has been nominated to be checked for its <a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Neutral_point_of_view" title="Wikipedia:Neutral point of view">neutrality</a></b>.<span class="hide-when-compact"> Discussion of this nomination can be found on the <a href="/wiki/Talk:Francis_Crick" title="Talk:Francis Crick">talk page</a>.</span>  <small><i>(November 2015)</i></small><small class="hide-when-compact"><i> (<a href="/wiki/Help:Maintenance_template_removal" title="Help:Maintenance template removal">Learn how and when to remove this template message</a>)</i></small></div></td></tr></tbody></table>\n<p>An enduring controversy has been generated by Watson and Crick\'s use of <a href="/wiki/Photo_51" title="Photo 51">DNA X-ray diffraction data</a> collected by Franklin and Wilkins. The controversy arose from the fact that some of Franklin\'s unpublished data were used without her knowledge or consent by Watson and Crick in their construction of the double helix model of DNA.<sup id="cite_ref-Profile_35-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Profile-35">&#91;35&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Judson_63-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Judson-63">&#91;63&#93;</a></sup>  Of the four DNA researchers, only Franklin had a degree in chemistry;<sup id="cite_ref-Profile_35-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Profile-35">&#91;35&#93;</a></sup> Wilkins and Crick had backgrounds in physics, Watson in biology.\n</p><p>Prior to publication of the double helix structure, Watson and Crick had little direct interaction with Franklin herself. They were, however, aware of her work, more aware than she herself realized. Watson was present at a lecture, given in November 1951, where Franklin presented the two forms of the molecule, type A and type B, and discussed the position of the phosphate units on the external part of the molecule. She also specified the amount of water to be found in the molecule in accordance with other parts of it, data that have considerable importance in terms of the stability of the molecule. She was the first to discover and formulate these facts, which in fact constituted the basis for all later attempts to build a model of the molecule.  Before this, both Linus Pauling and Watson and Crick had generated erroneous models with the chains inside and the bases pointing outwards.<sup id="cite_ref-ReferenceA_64-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ReferenceA-64">&#91;64&#93;</a></sup> Her identification of the <a href="/wiki/Space_group" title="Space group">space group</a> for DNA crystals revealed to Crick that the two DNA strands were <a href="/wiki/Antiparallel_(biochemistry)" title="Antiparallel (biochemistry)">antiparallel</a>.\n</p><p>In January 1953, Watson was shown an X-ray photograph of B-DNA (called <a href="/wiki/Photo_51" title="Photo 51">photograph 51</a>),<sup id="cite_ref-65" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-65">&#91;65&#93;</a></sup> by Wilkins.<sup id="cite_ref-Maddox_66-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Maddox-66">&#91;66&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-67" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-67">&#91;67&#93;</a></sup> Wilkins had been given photograph 51 by Rosalind Franklin\'s Ph.D. student Raymond Gosling.<sup id="cite_ref-Maddox_66-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Maddox-66">&#91;66&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-68" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-68">&#91;68&#93;</a></sup>  Wilkins and Gosling had worked together in the Medical Research Council\'s (MRC) Biophysics Unit before director John Randall appointed Franklin to take over both DNA diffraction work and guidance of Gosling\'s thesis. It appears that Randall did not communicate effectively with them about Franklin\'s appointment, contributing to confusion and friction between Wilkins and Franklin.<sup id="cite_ref-69" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-69">&#91;69&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>In the middle of February 1953, Crick\'s thesis advisor, Max Perutz, gave Crick <a href="/wiki/King%27s_College_London_DNA_Controversy" class="mw-redirect" title="King&#39;s College London DNA Controversy">a copy of a report</a> written for a Medical Research Council biophysics committee visit to King\'s in December 1952, containing data from the King\'s group, including some of Franklin\'s crystallographic calculations.<sup id="cite_ref-70" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-70">&#91;70&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-JudsonCh3_71-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-JudsonCh3-71">&#91;71&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-72" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-72">&#91;72&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-73" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-73">&#91;73&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Franklin was unaware that photograph 51 and other information had been shared with Crick and Watson. She wrote a series of three draft manuscripts, two of which included a double helical DNA backbone. Her two A form manuscripts reached Acta Crystallographica in Copenhagen on 6 March 1953,<sup id="cite_ref-74" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-74">&#91;74&#93;</a></sup> one day before Crick and Watson had completed their model.<sup id="cite_ref-75" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-75">&#91;75&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>The X-ray diffraction images collected by Gosling and Franklin provided the best evidence for the helical nature of DNA. Franklin\'s experimental work thus proved crucial in Watson and Crick\'s discovery. Her experimental results provided estimates of the water content of DNA crystals, and these results were most consistent with the three sugar-phosphate backbones being on the outside of the molecule.<sup id="cite_ref-76" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-76">&#91;76&#93;</a></sup> Franklin\'s X-Ray photograph showed that the backbones had to be on the outside. Although she at first insisted vehemently that her data did not force one to conclude that DNA has a helical structure, in the drafts she submitted in 1953 she argues for a double helical DNA backbone.  Her identification of the <a href="/wiki/Space_group" title="Space group">space group</a> for DNA crystals revealed to Crick that the DNA strands were <a href="/wiki/Antiparallel_(biochemistry)" title="Antiparallel (biochemistry)">antiparallel</a>, which helped Watson and Crick decide to look for DNA models with two antiparallel polynucleotide strands.\n</p><p>In summary, Watson and Crick had three sources for Franklin\'s unpublished data: 1) her 1951 seminar, attended by Watson,<sup id="cite_ref-77" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-77">&#91;77&#93;</a></sup> 2) discussions with Wilkins,<sup id="cite_ref-78" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-78">&#91;78&#93;</a></sup> who worked in the same laboratory with Franklin, 3) a research progress report that was intended to promote coordination of Medical Research Council-supported laboratories.<sup id="cite_ref-79" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-79">&#91;79&#93;</a></sup> Watson, Crick, Wilkins and Franklin all worked in MRC laboratories.\n</p><p>Crick and Watson felt that they had benefited from collaborating with Wilkins. They offered him a co-authorship on the article that first described the double helix structure of DNA. Wilkins turned down the offer, a fact that may have led to the terse character of the acknowledgement of experimental work done at King\'s College in the eventual published paper. Rather than make any of the DNA researchers at King\'s College co-authors on the Watson and Crick double helix article, the solution that was arrived at was to publish two additional papers from King\'s College along with the helix paper. <a href="/wiki/Brenda_Maddox" title="Brenda Maddox">Brenda Maddox</a> suggests that because of the importance of her experimental results in Watson and Crick\'s model building and theoretical analysis, Franklin should have had her name on the original Watson and Crick paper in <i><a href="/wiki/Nature_(journal)" title="Nature (journal)">Nature</a></i>.<sup id="cite_ref-MaddoxDLD_80-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-MaddoxDLD-80">&#91;80&#93;</a></sup> Franklin and Gosling submitted their own joint \'second\' paper to <i>Nature</i> at the same time as Wilkins, Stokes, and Wilson submitted theirs (i.e. the \'third\' paper on DNA).\n</p><p>Watson\'s portrayal of Franklin in <i><a href="/wiki/The_Double_Helix" title="The Double Helix">The Double Helix</a></i> (written after Franklin\'s death when libel laws did not apply anymore) was negative and gave the appearance that she was Wilkins\' assistant and was unable to interpret her own DNA data.<sup id="cite_ref-81" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-81">&#91;81&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>The X-ray diffraction images collected by Franklin provided the best evidence for the helical nature of DNA. While Franklin\'s experimental work proved important to Crick and Watson\'s development of a correct model, she herself could not realize it at the time. When she left King\'s College, Director Sir John Randall insisted that all DNA work belonged exclusively to King\'s and ordered Franklin to not even think about it.<sup id="cite_ref-82" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-82">&#91;82&#93;</a></sup>  Franklin subsequently did superb work in J. D. Bernal\'s Lab at Birkbeck College with the tobacco mosaic virus extending ideas on helical construction.<sup id="cite_ref-Profile_35-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Profile-35">&#91;35&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Crick was often described as very talkative, with Watson \xe2\x80\x93 in <i>The Double Helix</i> \xe2\x80\x93 implying lack of modesty.<sup id="cite_ref-83" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-83">&#91;83&#93;</a></sup> His personality combined with his scientific accomplishments produced many opportunities for Crick to stimulate reactions from others, both inside and outside the scientific world, which was the centre of his intellectual and professional life.<sup id="cite_ref-84" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-84">&#91;84&#93;</a></sup> Crick spoke rapidly, and rather loudly, and had an infectious and reverberating laugh, and a lively sense of humour. One colleague from the Salk Institute described him as "a brainstorming intellectual powerhouse with a mischievous smile.... Francis was never mean-spirited, just incisive. He detected microscopic flaws in logic.
