In a room full of smart scientists, Francis continually reearned his position as the heavyweight champ."<sup id="cite_ref-85" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-85">&#91;85&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Eugenics">Eugenics</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Francis_Crick&amp;action=edit&amp;section=9" title="Edit section: Eugenics">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<p>Crick occasionally expressed his views on <a href="/wiki/Eugenics" title="Eugenics">eugenics</a>, usually in private letters. For example, Crick advocated a form of <a href="/wiki/Liberal_eugenics" class="mw-redirect" title="Liberal eugenics">positive eugenics</a> in which wealthy parents would be encouraged to have more children.<sup id="cite_ref-86" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-86">&#91;86&#93;</a></sup> He once remarked, "In the long run, it is unavoidable that society will begin to worry about the character of the next generation... It is not a subject at the moment which we can tackle easily because people have so many religious beliefs and until we have a more uniform view of ourselves I think it would be risky to try and do anything in the way of eugenics... I would be astonished if, in the next 100 or 200&#160;years, society did not come round to the view that they would have to try to improve the next generation in some extent or one way or another."\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Creationism">Creationism</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Francis_Crick&amp;action=edit&amp;section=10" title="Edit section: Creationism">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<p>Crick was a firm critic of <a href="/wiki/Young_Earth_creationism" title="Young Earth creationism">Young Earth creationism</a>. In the 1987 <a href="/wiki/United_States_Supreme_Court" class="mw-redirect" title="United States Supreme Court">United States Supreme Court</a> case <i><a href="/wiki/Edwards_v._Aguillard" title="Edwards v. Aguillard">Edwards v. Aguillard</a></i>, Crick joined a group of other <a href="/wiki/Nobel_laureate" class="mw-redirect" title="Nobel laureate">Nobel laureates</a> who advised, "\'Creation-science\' simply has no place in the public-school science classroom."<sup id="cite_ref-87" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-87">&#91;87&#93;</a></sup> Crick was also an advocate for the establishment of <a href="/wiki/Darwin_Day" title="Darwin Day">Darwin Day</a> as a British national holiday.<sup id="cite_ref-88" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-88">&#91;88&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Views_on_religion">Views on religion</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Francis_Crick&amp;action=edit&amp;section=11" title="Edit section: Views on religion">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<p>Crick referred to himself as a humanist, which he defined as the belief "that human problems can and must be faced in terms of human moral and intellectual resources without invoking supernatural authority."  He publicly called for humanism to replace religion as a guiding force for humanity, writing:\n</p>\n<blockquote><p>The human dilemma is hardly new.  We find ourselves through no wish of our own on this slowly revolving planet in an obscure corner of a vast universe.  Our questioning intelligence will not let us live in cow-like content with our lot.  We have a deep need to know why we are here.  What is the world made of?  More important, what are we made of?  In the past religion answered these questions, often in considerable detail.  Now we know that almost all these answers are highly likely to be nonsense, having sprung from man\'s ignorance and his enormous capacity for self-deception... The simple fables of the religions of the world have come to seem like tales told to children.  Even understood symbolically they are often perverse, if not rather unpleasant... Humanists, then, live in a mysterious, exciting and intellectually expanding world, which, once glimpsed, makes the old worlds of the religions seem fake-cosy and stale... <sup id="cite_ref-89" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-89">&#91;89&#93;</a></sup></p></blockquote>\n<p>Crick was especially critical of <a href="/wiki/Christianity" title="Christianity">Christianity</a>:\n</p>\n<blockquote><p>I do not respect Christian beliefs.  I think they are ridiculous.  If we could get rid of them we could more easily get down to the serious problem of trying to find out what the world is all about.<sup id="cite_ref-90" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-90">&#91;90&#93;</a></sup></p></blockquote>\n<p>Crick once joked, "Christianity may be OK between consenting adults in private but should not be taught to young children."<sup id="cite_ref-guardianbooks_91-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-guardianbooks-91">&#91;91&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>In his book <i>Of Molecules and Men</i>, Crick expressed his views on the <a href="/wiki/Relationship_between_science_and_religion" class="mw-redirect" title="Relationship between science and religion">relationship between science and religion</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-92" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-92">&#91;92&#93;</a></sup> After suggesting that it would become possible for a <a href="/wiki/Computer" title="Computer">computer</a> to be programmed so as to have a <a href="/wiki/Soul_(spirit)" class="mw-redirect" title="Soul (spirit)">soul</a>, he wondered: at what point during biological evolution did the first organism have a soul? At what moment does a baby get a soul? Crick stated his view that the idea of a non-material soul that could enter a body and then persist after death is just that, an imagined idea. For Crick, the mind is a product of physical brain activity and the brain had evolved by natural means over millions of years. He felt that it was important that evolution by <a href="/wiki/Natural_selection" title="Natural selection">natural selection</a> be taught in schools and that it was regrettable that English schools had compulsory religious instruction. He also considered that a new scientific world view was rapidly being established, and predicted that once the detailed workings of the brain were eventually revealed, erroneous <a href="/wiki/Christianity" title="Christianity">Christian</a> concepts about the nature of humans and the world would no longer be tenable; traditional conceptions of the "soul" would be replaced by a new understanding of the physical basis of mind. He was sceptical of <a href="/wiki/Organized_religion" title="Organized religion">organized religion</a>, referring to himself as a sceptic and an agnostic with "a strong inclination towards atheism".<sup id="cite_ref-CrickWMP10_93-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-CrickWMP10-93">&#91;93&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>In 1960, Crick accepted an honorary fellowship at <a href="/wiki/Churchill_College,_Cambridge" title="Churchill College, Cambridge">Churchill College, Cambridge</a>, one factor being that the new college did not have a chapel. Some time later a large donation was made to establish a chapel and the College Council decided to accept it. Crick resigned his fellowship in protest.<sup id="cite_ref-pmid15151106_94-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-pmid15151106-94">&#91;94&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-95" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-95">&#91;95&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>In October 1969 Crick participated in a celebration of the 100th year of the journal <i><a href="/wiki/Nature_(journal)" title="Nature (journal)">Nature</a></i> in which he attempted to make some predictions about what the next 30&#160;years would hold for molecular biology. His speculations were later published in <i>Nature</i>.<sup id="cite_ref-Nature_1970_96-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Nature_1970-96">&#91;96&#93;</a></sup> Near the end of the article, Crick briefly mentioned the search for life on other planets, but he held little hope that <a href="/wiki/Extraterrestrial_life" title="Extraterrestrial life">extraterrestrial life</a> would be found by the year 2000. He also discussed what he described as a possible new direction for research, what he called "biochemical theology". Crick wrote "so many people pray that one finds it hard to believe that they do not get some satisfaction from it".<sup id="cite_ref-Nature_1970_96-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Nature_1970-96">&#91;96&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Crick suggested that it might be possible to find chemical changes in the brain that were molecular correlates of the act of <a href="/wiki/Prayer" title="Prayer">prayer</a>. He speculated that there might be a detectable change in the level of some <a href="/wiki/Neurotransmitter" title="Neurotransmitter">neurotransmitter</a> or <a href="/wiki/Neurohormone" title="Neurohormone">neurohormone</a> when people pray. He might have been imagining substances such as <a href="/wiki/Dopamine#Motivation_and_pleasure" title="Dopamine">dopamine</a> that are released by the brain under certain conditions and produce rewarding sensations. Crick\'s suggestion that there might someday be a new science of "biochemical theology" seems to have been realized under an alternative name: there is now the new field of <a href="/wiki/Neurotheology" class="mw-redirect" title="Neurotheology">neurotheology</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-97" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-97">&#91;97&#93;</a></sup> Crick\'s view of the relationship between science and religion continued to play a role in his work as he made the transition from <a href="/wiki/Molecular_biology" title="Molecular biology">molecular biology</a> research into theoretical neuroscience.\n</p><p>Crick asked in 1998 "and if some of the Bible is manifestly wrong, why should any of the rest of it be accepted automatically? ... And what would be more important than to find our true place in the universe by removing one by one these unfortunate vestiges of earlier beliefs?"<sup id="cite_ref-CrickWMP11_98-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-CrickWMP11-98">&#91;98&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>In 2003 he was one of 22 Nobel laureates who signed the <a href="/wiki/Humanism_and_Its_Aspirations" title="Humanism and Its Aspirations"><i>Humanist Manifesto</i></a>.<sup id="cite_ref-99" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-99">&#91;99&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Directed_panspermia">Directed panspermia</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Francis_Crick&amp;action=edit&amp;section=12" title="Edit section: Directed panspermia">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<p>During the 1960s, Crick became concerned with the origins of the genetic code. In 1966, Crick took the place of <a href="/wiki/Leslie_Orgel" title="Leslie Orgel">Leslie Orgel</a> at a meeting where Orgel was to talk about the <a href="/wiki/Origin_of_life" class="mw-redirect" title="Origin of life">origin of life</a>. Crick speculated about possible stages by which an initially simple code with a few amino acid types might have evolved into the more complex code used by existing <a href="/wiki/Organism" title="Organism">organisms</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-100" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-100">&#91;100&#93;</a></sup> At that time, everyone thought of <a href="/wiki/Protein" title="Protein">proteins</a> as the only kind of <a href="/wiki/Enzyme" title="Enzyme">enzymes</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Ribozyme" title="Ribozyme">ribozymes</a> had not yet been found. Many molecular biologists were puzzled by the problem of the origin of a protein replicating system that is as complex as that which exists in organisms currently inhabiting <a href="/wiki/Earth" title="Earth">Earth</a>. In the early 1970s, Crick and Orgel further speculated about the possibility that the production of living systems from <a href="/wiki/Molecule" title="Molecule">molecules</a> may have been a very rare event in the <a href="/wiki/Universe" title="Universe">universe</a>, but once it had developed it could be spread by intelligent life forms using <a href="/wiki/Spaceflight" title="Spaceflight">space travel</a> technology, a process they called "<a href="/wiki/Directed_panspermia" title="Directed panspermia">directed panspermia</a>".<sup id="cite_ref-101" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-101">&#91;101&#93;</a></sup> In a retrospective article,<sup id="cite_ref-CrickRetro_102-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-CrickRetro-102">&#91;102&#93;</a></sup> Crick and Orgel noted that they had been overly pessimistic about the chances of <a href="/wiki/Abiogenesis" title="Abiogenesis">abiogenesis</a> on Earth when they had assumed that some kind of self-replicating protein system was the molecular origin of life.\n</p><p>In 1976 Crick addressed the origin of protein synthesis in a paper with <a href="/wiki/Sydney_Brenner" title="Sydney Brenner">Sydney Brenner</a>, <a href="/wiki/Aaron_Klug" title="Aaron Klug">Aaron Klug</a>, and <a href="/w/index.php?title=George_Pieczenik&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" class="new" title="George Pieczenik (page does not exist)">George Pieczenik</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-103" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-103">&#91;103&#93;</a></sup> In this paper, they speculate that code constraints on nucleotide sequences allow protein synthesis without the need for a <a href="/wiki/Ribosome" title="Ribosome">ribosome</a>. It, however, requires a five base binding between the mRNA and tRNA with a flip of the anti-codon creating a triplet coding, even though it is a five-base physical interaction. <a href="/wiki/Thomas_H._Jukes" title="Thomas H. Jukes">Thomas H. Jukes</a> pointed out that the code constraints on the mRNA sequence required for this translation mechanism is still preserved.<sup id="cite_ref-104" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-104">&#91;104&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Neuroscience_and_other_interests">Neuroscience and other interests</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Francis_Crick&amp;action=edit&amp;section=13" title="Edit section: Neuroscience and other interests">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Decisionbrain.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a3/Decisionbrain.jpg/220px-Decisionbrain.jpg" width="220" height="130" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a3/Decisionbrain.jpg/330px-Decisionbrain.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/a/a3/Decisionbrain.jpg 2x" data-file-width="373" data-file-height="221" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Decisionbrain.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Results from an <a href="/wiki/FMRI" class="mw-redirect" title="FMRI">fMRI</a> experiment in which people made a conscious decision about a visual stimulus. The small region of the brain coloured orange shows patterns of activity that correlate with the decision making process. Crick stressed the importance of finding new methods to probe human brain function.</div></div></div>\n<p>Crick\'s period at Cambridge was the pinnacle of his long scientific career, but he left Cambridge in 1977 after 30&#160;years, having been offered (and having refused) the Mastership of <a href="/wiki/Gonville_and_Caius_College,_Cambridge" title="Gonville and Caius College, Cambridge">Gonville and Caius</a>. James Watson claimed at a Cambridge conference marking the 50th anniversary of the discovery of the structure of DNA in 2003:\n</p>\n<blockquote><p>Now perhaps it\'s a pretty well kept secret that one of the most uninspiring acts of the University of Cambridge over this past century was to turn down Francis Crick when he applied to be the <a href="/wiki/Arthur_Balfour_Professor_of_Genetics" title="Arthur Balfour Professor of Genetics">Professor of Genetics</a>, in 1958. Now there may have been a series of arguments, which led them to reject Francis. It was really saying, don\'t push us to the frontier.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (November 2007)">citation needed</span></a></i>&#93;</sup></p></blockquote>\n<p>The apparently "pretty well kept secret" had already been recorded in Soraya De Chadarevian\'s <i>Designs For Life: Molecular Biology After World War II</i>, published by <a href="/wiki/Cambridge_University_Press" title="Cambridge University Press">Cambridge University Press</a> in 2002. His major contribution to molecular biology in Cambridge is well documented in <i>The History of the University of Cambridge: Volume 4 (1870 to 1990)</i>, which was published by CUP in 1992.\n</p><p>According to the <a href="/wiki/University_of_Cambridge" title="University of Cambridge">University of Cambridge</a>\'s genetics department official website, the electors of the professorship could not reach consensus, prompting the intervention of then University <a href="/wiki/Vice-Chancellor" class="mw-redirect" title="Vice-Chancellor">Vice-Chancellor</a> <a href="/wiki/Edgar_Adrian,_1st_Baron_Adrian" class="mw-redirect" title="Edgar Adrian, 1st Baron Adrian">Lord Adrian</a>. Lord Adrian first offered the professorship to a compromise candidate, <a href="/wiki/Guido_Pontecorvo" title="Guido Pontecorvo">Guido Pontecorvo</a>, who refused, and is said to have offered it then to Crick, who also refused.\n</p><p>In 1976, Crick took a <a href="/wiki/Sabbatical" title="Sabbatical">sabbatical year</a> at the Salk Institute for Biological Studies in <a href="/wiki/La_Jolla,_California" class="mw-redirect" title="La Jolla, California">La Jolla, California</a>. Crick had been a nonresident fellow of the Institute since 1960. Crick wrote, "I felt at home in Southern California."<sup id="cite_ref-CrickWMP145_105-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-CrickWMP145-105">&#91;105&#93;</a></sup> After the sabbatical, Crick left Cambridge in order to continue working at the Salk Institute. He was also an adjunct professor at the <a href="/wiki/University_of_California,_San_Diego" title="University of California, San Diego">University of California, San Diego</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-106" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-106">&#91;106&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-107" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-107">&#91;107&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-108" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-108">&#91;108&#93;</a></sup> He taught himself <a href="/wiki/Neuroanatomy" title="Neuroanatomy">neuroanatomy</a> and studied many other areas of <a href="/wiki/Neuroscience" title="Neuroscience">neuroscience</a> research. It took him several years to disengage from molecular biology because exciting discoveries continued to be made, including the discovery of <a href="/wiki/Alternative_splicing" title="Alternative splicing">alternative splicing</a> and the discovery of <a href="/wiki/Restriction_enzyme" title="Restriction enzyme">restriction enzymes</a>, which helped make possible <a href="/wiki/Genetic_engineering" title="Genetic engineering">genetic engineering</a>. Eventually, in the 1980s, Crick was able to devote his full attention to his other interest, <a href="/wiki/Consciousness" title="Consciousness">consciousness</a>. His <a href="/wiki/Autobiography" title="Autobiography">autobiographical</a> book, <i><a href="/wiki/What_Mad_Pursuit:_A_Personal_View_of_Scientific_Discovery" class="mw-redirect" title="What Mad Pursuit: A Personal View of Scientific Discovery">What Mad Pursuit: A Personal View of Scientific Discovery</a></i>, includes a description of why he left molecular biology and switched to neuroscience.\n</p><p>Upon taking up work in theoretical neuroscience, Crick was struck by several things:\n</p>\n<ul><li>there were many isolated subdisciplines within neuroscience with little contact between them</li>\n<li>many people who were interested in behaviour treated the brain as a <a href="/wiki/Black_box_(systems)" class="mw-redirect" title="Black box (systems)">black box</a></li>\n<li>consciousness was viewed as a <a href="/wiki/Taboo" title="Taboo">taboo</a> subject by many <a href="/wiki/Neurobiologist" class="mw-redirect" title="Neurobiologist">neurobiologists</a></li></ul>\n<p>Crick hoped he might aid progress in neuroscience by promoting constructive interactions between specialists from the many different subdisciplines concerned with consciousness. He even collaborated with <a href="/wiki/Neurophilosophy" title="Neurophilosophy">neurophilosophers</a> such as <a href="/wiki/Patricia_Churchland" title="Patricia Churchland">Patricia Churchland</a>. In 1983, as a result of their studies of computer models of neural networks, Crick and Mitchison proposed that the function of <a href="/wiki/REM_sleep" class="mw-redirect" title="REM sleep">REM sleep</a> is to remove certain modes of interactions in networks of cells in the mammalian cerebral cortex; they called this hypothetical process \'<a href="/wiki/Reverse_learning" title="Reverse learning">reverse learning</a>\' or \'unlearning\'. In the final phase of his career, Crick established a collaboration with <a href="/wiki/Christof_Koch" title="Christof Koch">Christof Koch</a> that lead to publication of a series of articles on consciousness during the period spanning from 1990<sup id="cite_ref-109" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-109">&#91;109&#93;</a></sup> to 2005. Crick made the strategic decision to focus his theoretical investigation of consciousness on how the brain generates visual <a href="/wiki/Awareness" title="Awareness">awareness</a> within a few hundred milliseconds of viewing a scene. Crick and Koch proposed that consciousness seems so mysterious because it involves very short-term <a href="/wiki/Memory" title="Memory">memory</a> processes that are as yet poorly understood. Crick also published a book describing how <a href="/wiki/Neurobiology" class="mw-redirect" title="Neurobiology">neurobiology</a> had reached a mature enough stage so that consciousness could be the subject of a unified effort to study it at the molecular, cellular and behavioural levels. Crick\'s book <i><a href="/wiki/The_Astonishing_Hypothesis" title="The Astonishing Hypothesis">The Astonishing Hypothesis</a></i> made the argument that neuroscience now had the tools required to begin a scientific study of how <a href="/wiki/Human_brain" title="Human brain">brains</a> produce conscious experiences. Crick was sceptical about the value of <a href="/wiki/Connectionism" title="Connectionism">computational models</a> of mental function that are not based on details about brain structure and function.\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Awards_and_honours">Awards and honours</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Francis_Crick&amp;action=edit&amp;section=14" title="Edit section: Awards and honours">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:152px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Crick-stainedglass-gonville-caius.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9a/Crick-stainedglass-gonville-caius.jpg/150px-Crick-stainedglass-gonville-caius.jpg" width="150" height="293" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9a/Crick-stainedglass-gonville-caius.jpg/225px-Crick-stainedglass-gonville-caius.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9a/Crick-stainedglass-gonville-caius.jpg/300px-Crick-stainedglass-gonville-caius.jpg 2x" data-file-width="446" data-file-height="871" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Crick-stainedglass-gonville-caius.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Stained glass window in the dining hall of <a href="/wiki/Gonville_and_Caius_College,_Cambridge" title="Gonville and Caius College, Cambridge">Caius College</a>, in Cambridge, commemorating Francis Crick and representing the double helical structure of <a href="/wiki/B-DNA" class="mw-redirect" title="B-DNA">B-DNA</a>.</div></div></div>\n<p>In addition to his third share of the 1962 Nobel prize for Physiology or Medicine, he received many awards and honours, including the Royal and Copley medals of the Royal Society (1972 and 1975), and also the Order of Merit (on 27 November 1991); he refused an offer of a CBE in 1963,<sup id="cite_ref-110" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-110">&#91;110&#93;</a></sup> but was often referred to in error as \'Sir Francis Crick\' and even on occasions as \'Lord Crick.\' He was elected an <a href="/wiki/EMBO_Member" class="mw-redirect" title="EMBO Member">EMBO Member</a> in 1964.<sup id="cite_ref-membo_3-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-membo-3">&#91;3&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>The award of Nobel prizes to John Kendrew and Max Perutz, and to Crick, Watson, and Wilkins was satirised in a short sketch in the BBC TV programme <i><a href="/wiki/That_Was_The_Week_That_Was" class="mw-redirect" title="That Was The Week That Was">That Was The Week That Was</a></i> with the Nobel Prizes being referred to as \'The Alfred Nobel Peace Pools.\'\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Francis_Crick_Medal_and_Lecture">Francis Crick Medal and Lecture</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Francis_Crick&amp;action=edit&amp;section=15" title="Edit section: Francis Crick Medal and Lecture">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<p>The <a href="/wiki/Crick_Lecture" title="Crick Lecture">Francis Crick Medal and Lecture</a><sup id="cite_ref-111" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-111">&#91;111&#93;</a></sup> was established in 2003 following an endowment by his former colleague, <a href="/wiki/Sydney_Brenner" title="Sydney Brenner">Sydney Brenner</a>, joint winner of the 2002 Nobel Prize in Physiology and Medicine.<sup id="cite_ref-112" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-112">&#91;112&#93;</a></sup> The lecture is delivered annually in any field of biological sciences, with preference given to the areas in which Francis Crick himself worked. Importantly, the lectureship is aimed at younger scientists, ideally under 40, or whose career progression corresponds to this age. As of  2015<sup class="plainlinks noexcerpt noprint asof-tag update" style="display:none;"><a class="external text" href="//en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Francis_Crick&amp;action=edit">&#91;update&#93;</a></sup>, Crick lectures have been delivered by <a href="/wiki/Julie_Ahringer" title="Julie Ahringer">Julie Ahringer</a>, <a href="/wiki/Dario_Alessi" title="Dario Alessi">Dario Alessi</a>, <a href="/wiki/Ewan_Birney" title="Ewan Birney">Ewan Birney</a>, <a href="/wiki/Simon_Boulton" title="Simon Boulton">Simon Boulton</a>, Jason Chin, <a href="/wiki/Simon_Fisher" title="Simon Fisher">Simon Fisher</a>, Matthew Hurles, <a href="/wiki/Gilean_McVean" title="Gilean McVean">Gilean McVean</a>, <a href="/wiki/Duncan_Odom" title="Duncan Odom">Duncan Odom</a>, <a href="/wiki/Geraint_Rees" title="Geraint Rees">Geraint Rees</a>, <a href="/wiki/Sarah_Teichmann" title="Sarah Teichmann">Sarah Teichmann</a> and <a href="/wiki/Daniel_Wolpert" title="Daniel Wolpert">Daniel Wolpert</a>.\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Francis_Crick_Institute">Francis Crick Institute</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Francis_Crick&amp;action=edit&amp;section=16" title="Edit section: Francis Crick Institute">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<p>The <a href="/wiki/Francis_Crick_Institute" title="Francis Crick Institute">Francis Crick Institute</a> is a \xc2\xa3660 million biomedical research centre located in north <a href="/wiki/London" title="London">London</a>, <a href="/wiki/United_Kingdom" title="United Kingdom">United Kingdom</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-guardianplans_113-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-guardianplans-113">&#91;113&#93;</a></sup> The Francis Crick Institute is a partnership between <a href="/wiki/Cancer_Research_UK" title="Cancer Research UK">Cancer Research UK</a>, <a href="/wiki/Imperial_College_London" title="Imperial College London">Imperial College London</a>, King\'s College London, the Medical Research Council, University College London (UCL) and the <a href="/wiki/Wellcome_Trust" title="Wellcome Trust">Wellcome Trust</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-the15411_114-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-the15411-114">&#91;114&#93;</a></sup> Completed in 2016, it is the largest centre for biomedical research and innovation in <a href="/wiki/Europe" title="Europe">Europe</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-guardianplans_113-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-guardianplans-113">&#91;113&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Francis_Crick_Graduate_Lectures">Francis Crick Graduate Lectures</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Francis_Crick&amp;action=edit&amp;section=17" title="Edit section: Francis Crick Graduate Lectures">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<p>The University of Cambridge Graduate School of Biological, Medical and Veterinary Sciences hosts The Francis Crick Graduate Lectures. The first two lectures were by <a href="/wiki/John_Gurdon" title="John Gurdon">John Gurdon</a> and <a href="/wiki/Tim_Hunt" title="Tim Hunt">Tim Hunt</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-115" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-115">&#91;115&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-116" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-116">&#91;116&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Other_honours">Other honours</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Francis_Crick&amp;action=edit&amp;section=18" title="Edit section: Other honours">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<ul><li>The inscription on the helices of a <a href="/wiki/DNA" title="DNA">DNA</a> sculpture (which was donated by James Watson) outside <a href="/wiki/Clare_College,_Cambridge" title="Clare College, Cambridge">Clare College</a>\'s Thirkill Court, Cambridge, England reads: <i> "The structure of DNA was discovered in 1953 by Francis Crick and James Watson while Watson lived here at Clare." </i> and on the base: <i>"The double helix model was supported by the work of Rosalind Franklin and Maurice Wilkins."</i></li>\n<li>Another sculpture entitled <i>Discovery</i>, by artist Lucy Glendinning was installed on Tuesday, 13 December 2005 in Abington Street, Northampton. According to the late Lynn Wilson, chairman of the Wilson Foundation, "The sculpture celebrates the life of a world class scientist who must surely be considered the greatest Northamptonian of all time&#160;\xe2\x80\x94 by discovering DNA he unlocked the whole future of genetics and the alphabet of life."</li>\n<li>Westminster City Council unveiled a green plaque to Francis Crick on the front fa\xc3\xa7ade of 56 St George\'s Square, Pimlico, London SW1 on 20 June 2007; Crick lived in the first floor flat, together with <a href="/wiki/Robert_Dougall" title="Robert Dougall">Robert Dougall</a> of BBC radio and later TV fame, a former Royal Navy associate.<sup id="cite_ref-117" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-117">&#91;117&#93;</a></sup></li>\n<li>In addition, Crick was elected a <a href="/wiki/List_of_Fellows_of_the_Royal_Society_elected_in_1959" title="List of Fellows of the Royal Society elected in 1959">Fellow of the Royal Society (FRS) in 1959</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-rsbm_1-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-rsbm-1">&#91;1&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-frs_2-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-frs-2">&#91;2&#93;</a></sup> a Fellow of the <a href="/wiki/International_Academy_of_Humanism" title="International Academy of Humanism">International Academy of Humanism</a>, and a Fellow of <a href="/wiki/CSICOP" class="mw-redirect" title="CSICOP">CSICOP</a>.</li>\n<li>At a meeting of the executive council of the <a href="/wiki/Committee_for_Skeptical_Inquiry" title="Committee for Skeptical Inquiry">Committee for Skeptical Inquiry (CSI)</a> (formerly CSICOP) in <a href="/wiki/Denver" title="Denver">Denver</a>, <a href="/wiki/Colorado" title="Colorado">Colorado</a> in April 2011, Crick was selected for inclusion in CSI\'s Pantheon of Skeptics.  The Pantheon of Skeptics was created by CSI to remember the legacy of deceased fellows of CSI and their contributions to the cause of scientific scepticism.<sup id="cite_ref-118" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-118">&#91;118&#93;</a></sup></li>\n<li>A sculpted bust of Francis Crick by <a href="/wiki/John_Sherrill_Houser" title="John Sherrill Houser">John Sherrill Houser</a>, which incorporates a single \'Golden\' Helix, was cast in bronze in the artist\'s studio in New Mexico, US. The bronze was first displayed at the Francis Crick Memorial Conference (on Consciousness) at the University of Cambridge\'s Churchill College on 7 July 2012; it was bought by Mill Hill School in May 2013, and displayed at the inaugural Crick Dinner on 8 June 2013, and will be again at their Crick Centenary Dinner in 2016.</li>\n<li>The <a href="/wiki/Benjamin_Franklin_Medal_for_Distinguished_Achievement_in_the_Sciences" class="mw-redirect" title="Benjamin Franklin Medal for Distinguished Achievement in the Sciences">Benjamin Franklin Medal for Distinguished Achievement in the Sciences</a> of the <a href="/wiki/American_Philosophical_Society" title="American Philosophical Society">American Philosophical Society</a> (2001), together with Watson.<sup id="cite_ref-franklinscience_recipients_119-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-franklinscience_recipients-119">&#91;119&#93;</a></sup></li>\n<li>Crick featured in the BBC Radio 4 series <i><a href="/wiki/The_New_Elizabethans" title="The New Elizabethans">The New Elizabethans</a></i> to mark the <a href="/wiki/Diamond_Jubilee_of_Queen_Elizabeth_II" class="mw-redirect" title="Diamond Jubilee of Queen Elizabeth II">diamond Jubilee of Queen Elizabeth II</a> in 2012. A panel of seven academics, journalists and historians named Crick among a group of 60 people in the UK "whose actions during the reign of Elizabeth II have had a significant impact on lives in these islands and given the age its character".<sup id="cite_ref-120" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-120">&#91;120&#93;</a></sup></li></ul>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Books">Books</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Francis_Crick&amp;action=edit&amp;section=19" title="Edit section: Books">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<table class="vertical-navbox nowraplinks" style="float:right;clear:right;width:auto;margin:0 0 1.0em 1.0em;background:#f9f9f9;border:1px solid #aaa;padding:0.2em;border-spacing:0.4em 0;text-align:center;line-height:1.4em;font-size:88%;text-align:left;"><tbody><tr><td style="padding-top:0.4em;line-height:1.2em"><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:LIBRARY" class="mw-redirect" title="Wikipedia:LIBRARY">Library resources</a> about <br /> <b>Francis Crick</b> <hr /></td></tr><tr><td class="plainlist" style="padding:0 0.1em 0.4em">\n<ul><li><a class="external text" href="//tools.wmflabs.org/ftl/cgi-bin/ftl?st=viaf&amp;su=49223910">Resources in your library</a></li>\n<li><a class="external text" href="//tools.wmflabs.org/ftl/cgi-bin/ftl?st=viaf&amp;su=49223910&amp;library=0CHOOSE0">Resources in other libraries</a></li></ul></td>\n</tr><tr><th style="padding:0.1em">\nBy Francis Crick</th></tr><tr><td class="plainlist" style="padding:0 0.1em 0.4em">\n<ul><li><a class="external text" href="//tools.wmflabs.org/ftl/cgi-bin/ftl?at=viaf&amp;au=49223910">Resources in your library</a></li>\n<li><a class="external text" href="//tools.wmflabs.org/ftl/cgi-bin/ftl?at=viaf&amp;au=49223910&amp;library=0CHOOSE0">Resources in other libraries</a></li></ul></td>\n</tr></tbody></table>\n<ul><li><i>Of Molecules and Men</i> (Prometheus Books, 2004; Watson and Crick were open to the idea of tentatively ignoring all individual experimental results, in case they might be wrong or misleading. Judson describes how Watson spent a large amount of time <i>ignoring</i> Crick\'s belief (based on Franklin\'s determination of the space group) that the two backbone strands were antiparallel. On page 176, Judson quotes a letter written by Watson, "The model has been derived almost entirely from stereochemical considerations with the only X-ray consideration being the spacing between the pair of bases 3.4 A which was originally found by Astbury."</span>\n</li>\n<li id="cite_note-45"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-45">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">See Chapter 3 of <i>The Eighth Day of Creation: Makers of the Revolution in Biology</i> by Horace Freeland Judson published by Cold Spring Harbor Laboratory Press (1996) <a href="/wiki/International_Standard_Book_Number" title="International Standard Book Number">ISBN</a>&#160;<a href="/wiki/Special:BookSources/0-87969-478-5" title="Special:BookSources/0-87969-478-5">0-87969-478-5</a>. Judson also lists the publications of W. T. Astbury that described his early X-ray diffraction results for DNA.</span>\n</li>\n<li id="cite_note-WMP75-46"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-WMP75_46-0">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><a href="#Crick1990">Crick (1990)</a> p. 75: "If Jim had been killed by a tennis ball, I am reasonably sure I would not have solved the structure alone".</span>\n</li>\n<li id="cite_note-Simon,_Matthew_2005-47"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-Simon,_Matthew_2005_47-0">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">Simon, Matthew (2005) <i>Emergent Computation: emphasizing bioinformatics</i>. The first chapter of <a href="/wiki/Horace_Freeland_Judson" title="Horace Freeland Judson">Horace Judson</a>\'s book <i>The Eighth Day of Creation</i> describes the importance of Crick\'s talking and his boldness in his scientific style.</span>\n</li>\n<li id="cite_note-84"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-84">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">Describing Crick\'s influence on his scientific colleagues, Francis Crick Papers archivist Chris Beckett wrote of the importance of "...&#160;Crick\'s presence and eloquence&#160;\xe2\x80\x94direct and beguiling, by all accounts in the archive\xe2\x80\x94 at conference after conference, through formal lectures, extempore summaries, informal meetings and individual conversations. Indeed, one has the impression that it was through these frequent persuasive moments of personal delivery and purposive conversations that Crick was most influential."<br /><cite class="citation
